Title: Rubio meets Syrian counterpart in latest step towards normalizing relations
Description: <p>Secretary of State Marco Rubio met Thursday in Antalya, Turkey with Syrian Foreign Minister Asaad al-Shaibani, the State Department said. It's the first such meeting in 15 years.</p><p><strong>Why it matters: </strong>It's another step in normalizing relations between the countries after President Trump <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/14/trump-meets-syria-president-sharaa" target="_blank">met President Ahmed al-Sharaa</a> and made the shocking pronouncement that the U.S. would <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/13/trump-syria-al-sharaa-meeting-saudi" target="_blank">lift its crippling sanctions</a> on the country.</p><hr><ul><li>The last meeting between top diplomats from the two countries took place in 2010, when then-Secretary Hillary Clinton met her Syrian counterpart Walid Muallem in New York. </li><li>The Syrian civil war began the next year, and the U.S. shunned the Assad regime.</li></ul><p><strong>Driving the news:</strong> Rubio met his Syrian counterpart bilaterally and also held a trilateral meeting with Al-Shaibani and Turkish Foreign Minister Hakan Fidan.   </p><ul><li>Rubio told his Syrian counterpart the Trump administration would start the process of sanctions relief to stabilize Syria, State Department spokeswoman Tammy Bruce said. </li><li>Bruce said Rubio told his Syrian counterpart that he welcomes "the Syrian government's calls for peace with Israel, efforts to end Iran's influence in Syria, commitment to ascertaining the fate of U.S. citizens missing or killed in Syria, and elimination of all chemical weapons."</li><li>Rubio stressed the Syrian government must protect the human rights of all Syrians regardless of ethnicity or religion.</li></ul><p><strong>State of play:</strong> The Treasury Department said on Thursday that it's working with the State Department and National Security Council to execute <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/14/trump-syria-iran-gaza-ukraine-diplomacy-saudi-trip" target="_blank">Trump's direction</a> to lift sanctions.</p><ul><li>"We look forward to implementing the necessary authorizations that would be critical to bringing new investment into Syria," Treasury said.</li><li>Rubio said Thursday that Trump is expected to issue presidential waivers removing some of the U.S. sanctions against Syria.  </li><li>Other sanctions on Syria would have to be repealed by Congress, which is likely to take longer.</li></ul><p><strong>Between the lines:</strong> Ahead of the meeting in Antalya, Rubio spoke by phone with Israeli Prime Minister <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/13/edan-alexander-release-secret-talks-bahbah" target="_blank">Benjamin Netanyahu</a> and briefed him on Trump's meeting with al-Sharaa in Saudi Arabia.</p><ul><li>The Trump administration didn't notify Israel in advance of Trump's decision to hold that meeting and lift sanctions on Syria, a source with knowledge said. </li><li>During his meeting with Trump last month, Netanyahu specifically asked the president not to lift the sanctions and expressed concern about the role Turkey — which wanted the sanctions lifted — plays in Syria, Israeli officials say. </li><li>Trump disregarded Netanyahu's concerns. He said Tuesday that his decision to lift sanctions was in response to requests by several leaders including Saudi Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman, who joined the meeting with al-Sharaa, and Turkish President Recep Tayyip Erdoğan, who participated remotely.</li></ul><p><strong>Go deeper: </strong><a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/15/trump-israel-syria-policy-reverse-biden" target="_blank">Biden officials "awed" by Trump's rule-breaking Middle East moves</a></p>

Title: "State of the People Tour" seeks to organize Black voters into new coalition
Description: <p><a href="https://www.instagram.com/angelarye/?hl=en" target="_blank">Angela Rye</a> isn't waiting on Washington. She's betting on — and investing in — the people.</p><p><strong> Why it matters:</strong> Led by Rye and fueled by local organizers and national partners, the 10-city <a href="https://stateoftheppl.com/" target="_blank">State of the People Power Tour</a> aims to meet urgent needs while building lasting infrastructure in Black communities.</p><hr><p><strong>The big picture: </strong>A former Congressional Black Caucus adviser and co-host of <a href="https://www.youtube.com/%40NativeLandPod" target="_blank">Native Land Pod</a>, Rye studied past efforts like <a href="https://www.blackpast.org/african-american-history/gary-declaration-national-black-political-convention-1972/" target="_blank">the 1972 National Black Political Convention</a> in Gary, Indiana and <a href="https://www.history.com/this-day-in-history/october-16/million-man-march-1995" target="_blank">The Million Man March</a> — not just for inspiration, but to understand why so many agendas faded, and how to build something that lasts.</p><ul><li>"We keep doing this… but this time, we're not letting it die on the table." </li></ul><p><strong>The effort kicked</strong> off in metro <a href="https://www.ajc.com/news/atlanta-news/this-is-our-civil-rights-movement-nikema-williams-tells-decatur-rally/I3LPGLGFERDXNG7K3GM62NHRD4/" target="_blank">Atlanta in late April</a> and has made stops in Birmingham, Durham, Raleigh, and New Orleans — cities where Black communities face political pressure and economic precarity.</p><ul><li>Upcoming stops include Richmond on Friday and Saturday, Detroit (May 21-22), Jackson (May 30-31), Louisville (June 2-3) and Los Angeles (June 6-7).</li><li><a href="https://link.mediaoutreach.meltwater.com/ls/click?upn=u001.3KIwXUuHm3VZKH4Y-2FATZlwqJoNyItzEzDG-2Fbm-2BJsbh-2FpHNNCMhISV1wWsoVORN7uwcIjXL4PYREBNFGtN4D4FQ-3D-3DSVxg_RcQm0ktxT0kuk-2Fllr1pb0Q52n4Cak8z2eeOQ-2FDOUPvME-2Ft8rOexTEkd5dd89sQX4EMBisaX1jdpziIz6fOpOrBdAAbigBmsUn-2FjDskyRZk4xiIb3RksDQv1J2odMGj9QPvUb-2FXKVPpJHaqYKjNL3c7EF1ymS3Gr1x9Bblw68NeDJouzXXh231YKxpdc-2FhPiqnpKRP4krK3Sw5UgvUF-2BQuRXtPRsTmr5qoMlzPjuKby-2BfkZ1Y3UvA09oQPMZgf3t4uVHrQzMiTJO0KNA2-2BMiEjSS1Ge55tHH0oLKKh8y6DZr58b18mfHKQNyMqhuSHVL8KvtySBvD-2BhPV17FxYTI7zercwDJiqRzBpIuVr3AuEeXE9ElqSHsw-2F8jI27xqKuBspUkPu-2BDXctV0h9DmyiSK7A-3D-3D" target="_blank">David J. Johns</a><strong>, </strong>CEO and executive director of the National Black Justice Coalition (NBJC), one of the tour's leaders, tells Axios that the goal is to unite various coalitions fighting against President Trump's agenda.</li><li>"The goal is to really tap into that, to affirm for our folks that they're not crazy, that democracy has to be defended with each generation."</li></ul><p><strong>Context: </strong>The tour comes at a time when the political climate is increasingly hostile — <a href="https://www.axios.com/local/washington-dc/2025/01/23/trumps-diversity-rollback-sparks-civil-rights-outcry" target="_blank">diversity, equity and inclusion</a> (DEI) programs are gutted, <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/02/08/education-department-book-bans-censorship" target="_blank">book bans spread</a>, and <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/02/12/states-money-federal-government" target="_blank">federal relief dries up</a> — while many working families are still unable to meet basic needs.</p><ul><li>Organizers say these overlapping crises demand more than conversation.</li><li>"People want to be seen, supported and safe," she said. "If we don't build that together, who will?"</li></ul><p><strong>Case in point:</strong> In New Orleans, Rye says a young mother broke down at the registration table. She worked two jobs but still couldn't afford her electric bill — and showed up hoping the tour could help.</p><ul><li>"We're not just telling folks to vote while they starve," Rye told Axios. "We're helping with bills, jumping cars, and asking why full-time workers still can't keep the lights on."</li></ul><img src="https://images.axios.com/4YEPiZNip3KJJM9MhmSaGGgOdUo=/2025/05/14/1747243320482.jpg"> <div>Angela Rye at the North Carolina stop of the State of the People Power Tour. Photo:  Trav Cochran/HBCU Media</div><p><strong>Zoom in: </strong>The seeds of this effort sprouted weeks after <a href="https://www.instagram.com/reel/DGxkLpbxoU1/" target="_blank">a 24-hour livestream</a> driven on social media, but discussions that followed showed Rye the community needed something more: a fusion of crisis response and movement infrastructure — what Rye calls "holistic care."</p><ul><li>"We're not slicing up a pie here — we're planting a garden," said Wes Bellamy, political science chair at Virginia State University, who helped organize and bring the tour to Richmond. "We want to grow something that feeds people now and teaches them how to grow for themselves going forward."</li></ul><p><strong>That means more</strong> than voter registration. It means helping expunge records, feeding families, and activating a new kind of civic participation — one grounded in people's daily crises.</p><p><strong>The intrigue: </strong>At the heart of the tour is the <a href="https://link.mediaoutreach.meltwater.com/ls/click?upn=u001.3KIwXUuHm3VZKH4Y-2FATZl85IdrDhd-2FMwfi3GhF4BRKDcVa-2F1KGhpAwnTfKW1nxrs2cbO_RcQm0ktxT0kuk-2Fllr1pb0Q52n4Cak8z2eeOQ-2FDOUPvME-2Ft8rOexTEkd5dd89sQX4EMBisaX1jdpziIz6fOpOrBdAAbigBmsUn-2FjDskyRZk4xiIb3RksDQv1J2odMGj9QPvUb-2FXKVPpJHaqYKjNL3c7EF1ymS3Gr1x9Bblw68NeDJouzXXh231YKxpdc-2FhPiqnpKRP4krK3Sw5UgvUF-2BQuRXtPRsTmr5qoMlzPjuKby-2FQ1kfCKrh0ZGYBhkLiJ8CzWvpsr2ZI92Kh0rxltl15WfjEwPrm5tBGxKDi3LtYJUUKjv37fps6hiuzWoGTXkL4toMEIzSJkfi7-2FiwozAdHT5o1-2B6sNKS8zdC9Mtdh5JYN0uZsvCI9vuMcoRqaqvptZFFnQKrFl9ESJ7ce2wMf0EA-3D-3D" target="_blank">Black Papers Policy Project</a>, a series of over 20 comprehensive policy papers developed by more than 100 Black experts, offering solutions to the challenges facing Black communities.</p><ul><li>Johns said those papers range from national security, a Black economic vision, Black health equity, to charting pathways and opportunities in Black education and supporting Black veterans.</li></ul><p><strong>Zoom out:</strong> The tour is supported by over 200 local and national partners, including Black Voters Matter, Color of Change, and the NAACP. It grew from a March convening where organizers began shaping a modern Black agenda rooted in self-determination and long-term infrastructure.</p><ul><li>"Political outcomes are the result of community building," NAACP President Derrick Johnson told Axios. "And sometimes we conflate political transactions with movement work."</li></ul><img src="https://images.axios.com/cHbthuThbta2HpTnGU3E4lhUzFk=/2025/05/14/1747251940990.jpg"> <div>A scene from last month's New Orleans stop of the State of the People Power Tour. Photo courtesy of the tour organizers.</div><p><strong>Johnson praised the</strong> tour's focus on hyperlocal needs and emphasized that engagement doesn't always look the same.</p><ul><li>"Not everyone is going to be at a picket line," Johnson said. "But they may be losing benefits or unsure why policy changes are hurting them. The beauty of this tour is that it helps connect those dots — and those people."</li></ul><p><strong>Rye takes that further</strong>, pushing back on a <a href="https://medium.com/the-bad-influence/why-arent-black-people-protesting-f2f5e4b6a81f" target="_blank">growing narrative</a> that Black organizers and voters <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/04/12/missing-black-lives-matter-anti-trump-protests" target="_blank">have checked out</a>.  </p><ul><li>"We're not sitting this out. We've been <em>showing up,</em> surviving, strategizing—and now we're organizing for the long term."</li></ul><p><strong>Her message is clear:</strong> Stop waiting for saviors. Start showing up for each other.</p><p><strong>The bottom line: </strong>Rye said this has been about action and notes, "We're not letting it die in committee. This is about follow-through."</p>

Title: Supreme Court wrestles with limits on Trump's executive orders
Description: <p>The Supreme Court's conservative majority on Thursday seemed to want to rein in the spate of lower-court rulings that have frozen parts of <a href="https://www.axios.com/politics-policy/donald-trump" target="_blank">President Trump</a>'s agenda, but struggled with the right way to do it.</p><p><strong>The big picture: </strong>The justices heard oral arguments Thursday about whether to let Trump proceed with a plan to redefine <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/01/21/trump-birthright-citizenship-14th-amendment" target="_blank">birthright citizenship</a> in the U.S. or leave that policy frozen while the courts hash out whether it's legal.</p><hr><p><strong>Catch up quick: </strong>Trump signed an executive order on his first day in office that aims to <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/04/17/trump-birthright-citizenship-supreme-court" target="_blank">eliminate birthright citizenship</a> for children who are born in the U.S. to parents who are not in the country legally.</p><ul><li>The policy rests on a novel legal theory that would upend a century-long understanding of the Constitution's 14th Amendment.</li><li>Multiple federal courts <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/02/05/trump-birthright-citizenship-federal-judge-injunction" target="_blank">blocked</a> Trump from implementing the new policy until the courts determine whether it's legal. Multiple appeals courts left those temporary freezes in place. The Justice Department asked the Supreme Court to lift them.</li></ul><p><strong>Zoom out: </strong>The justices weren't debating whether Trump's policy is constitutional, but rather whether a single district court should be able to freeze an entire federal policy. </p><ul><li>Those courts' rulings should only apply to the specific parties before them, the Justice Department argued.</li><li>Solicitor General John Sauer told the justices that nationwide injunctions are a "bipartisan problem." Legal experts from both sides of the aisle have said they're <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/03/23/trump-boasberg-courts-constitutional-crisis" target="_blank">getting out of hand</a>.</li></ul><p><strong>What they're saying:</strong> Several of the court's conservative justices have expressed a desire to crack down on nationwide injunctions, but they wrestled Thursday with serious questions about the implications of letting Trump's citizenship order proceed.</p><ul><li>Liberal Justice Elena Kagan pressed Sauser on a hypothetical: What if the Supreme Court says lower courts cannot freeze the citizenship policy nationwide, but then ultimately rules that the policy is illegal?</li><li>In the time it took for the court to issue a final ruling, thousands of children would be denied citizenship, and would not be citizens of any country, she noted. And what if the Trump administration simply never asked the court to rule on the merits, she asked. In that case there would be no way to set a clear national standard for who's a citizen.</li></ul><p><strong>Chief Justice John Roberts,</strong> sometimes a swing vote on the court, seemed unmoved by those concerns. But they seemed to resonate with some other conservatives.</p><ul><li>Ultimately, Thursday's questioning didn't provide an especially clear picture of how the court is likely to rule.</li><li>That decision is expected by late June.</li></ul>

Title: FAA probes radio failure at Denver airport
Description: <p>The Federal Aviation Administration is investigating a Monday failure of air traffic controllers' radio at <a href="https://www.axios.com/local/denver/2025/02/19/denver-international-airport-great-hall-construction-completion-2027" target="_blank">Denver International Airport</a> — one of the <a href="https://www.denverpost.com/2025/04/15/denver-international-airport-rankings-worlds-busiest/" target="_blank">world's busiest airports</a>, per an FAA statement.</p><p><strong>Why it matters: </strong>It's the latest in a <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2025/05/12/briefing/air-travel-newark-chaos.html" target="_blank">string of alarming tech and safety lapses</a> nationwide that's shaking confidence in the U.S. aviation network.</p><hr><p><strong>The latest: </strong>Around 1:50pm Monday, a segment of the Denver Air Route Traffic Control Center lost communications for approximately 90 seconds when both primary transmitters went down, the FAA confirmed Thursday in a statement to Axios Denver.</p><ul><li>Air traffic controllers switched to an alternate frequency to direct planes.</li><li>The FAA said all aircraft remained safely separated and operations weren't affected.</li><li>Still, <a href="https://www.denver7.com/news/investigations/outage-leaves-up-to-20-pilots-headed-to-denver-airport-unable-to-contact-air-traffic-control-on-monday" target="_blank">Denver7 reports</a> as many as 20 aircraft flying toward DIA couldn't reach controllers for up to six minutes.</li></ul><p><strong>By the numbers: </strong>Assuming the planes were traveling at a groundspeed of 200 knots, they would have covered 20 miles without ATC contact in those six minutes.</p><p><strong>Between the lines: </strong>As of 2023, DIA's air traffic control tower was short seven controllers, and the facility that guides planes in the surrounding area was down 10, <a href="https://www.axios.com/local/denver/2025/02/12/colorado-s-significant-air-traffic-controller-shortages" target="_blank">a recent FAA report shows</a>.</p><p><strong>The big picture:</strong> The incident adds Denver to a growing list of major U.S. airports grappling with technology failures in the air traffic control system — an increasingly urgent concern amid a <a href="https://www.axios.com/local/denver/2023/08/07/global-entry-wait-times-denver-international-airport" target="_blank">post-pandemic travel boom</a>.</p><ul><li>The FAA has blamed <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/07/faa-updates-newark-philadelphia-flights-delays" target="_blank">recent mishaps</a> on aging infrastructure and critical staffing shortages.</li><li>Last week, U.S. Transportation Secretary Sean Duffy <a href="https://www.npr.org/2025/05/08/nx-s1-5391297/air-traffic-control-system-overhaul-modernization" target="_blank">unveiled a multi-billion-dollar modernization plan</a> aimed at upgrading outdated systems and improving reliability.</li></ul><p><strong>What they're saying: </strong>On Wednesday, DIA officials told Axios Denver: "We enjoy a strong partnership with the FAA and have full confidence in knowing that they put safety first in everything they do related to operations" at DIA. </p><ul><li>They declined to comment on Duffy's plan.</li></ul><p><strong>What's next:</strong> The FAA emphasized its findings are preliminary and said the investigation is ongoing.</p><p><strong>Go deeper: </strong><a href="https://www.axios.com/local/denver/2025/02/12/colorado-s-significant-air-traffic-controller-shortages" target="_blank">Colorado's significant air traffic controller shortages</a></p><p><em>Editor's note: This is a breaking story. Please check back for additional details.</em></p>

Title: What to know about birthright citizenship as it heads to Supreme Court
Description: <p>The Supreme Court said on Thursday that it will hear arguments next month over President <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/01/21/trump-birthright-citizenship-14th-amendment" target="_blank">Trump's efforts to restrict</a> birthright citizenship.</p><p><strong>Why it matters: </strong>Trump's bid to <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/01/23/trump-birthright-citizenship-judge-blocked" target="_blank">end the constitutionally guaranteed right</a> for some is the centerpiece of his administration's <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/02/04/trump-immigration-strategy-homeland-security" target="_blank">sweeping immigration crackdown</a>, in which he's already <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/04/15/kilmar-abrego-garcia-deported-case-return" target="_blank">defied the Supreme Court</a>.</p><hr><p><strong>State of play: </strong>The nation's highest court said it would hear oral arguments over the case on May 15.</p><p><strong>Here's what to know:</strong></p><h2>What is birthright citizenship?</h2><p>Birthright citizenship, as outlined by the <a href="https://www.axios.com/2024/12/10/trump-birthright-citizenship-executive-action" target="_blank">Constitution's 14th Amendment</a>, automatically confers citizenship to people born on U.S. soil – regardless of their parents' citizenship status.</p><ul><li>There are <a href="https://www.americanimmigrationcouncil.org/sites/default/files/research/birthright_citizenship_factsheet_241017.pdf" target="_blank">two types</a> in the U.S.: <em>jus sanguinis</em>, ancestry-based citizenship, and <em>jus soli</em>, birthplace-based citizenship.</li><li>In the former, which means "right of blood," children born abroad to at least one U.S. citizen parent <em>may be</em> entitled to U.S. citizenship, if they meet certain <a href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/8/1401" target="_blank">requirements</a>.</li><li><em>Jus soli</em>, on the other hand, is "the right of the soil" guaranteeing citizenship to almost everyone born in the U.S.</li></ul><p><strong>Zoom out: </strong>Birthright citizenship was added to the Constitution in the <a href="https://constitution.congress.gov/constitution/amendment-14/" target="_blank">14th Amendment</a> after the Civil War to guarantee citizenship for formerly enslaved people who were newly freed.</p><ul><li>The right was <a href="https://www.oyez.org/cases/1850-1900/169us649" target="_blank">affirmed</a> by the Supreme Court in the 1890s, cementing birthright citizenship for children born in the U.S. to parents who are not citizens.</li></ul><h2>What is Trump trying to change?</h2><p>Trump <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/01/21/trump-birthright-citizenship-14th-amendment" target="_blank">signed</a> an executive order on his first day in office this year, which seeks to end birthright citizenship for children born in the U.S. to noncitizen parents and undocumented immigrants.</p><h2>Has anyone fought these efforts?</h2><p>Trump's executive order was <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/02/20/trump-birthright-citizenship-order-blocked-court-appeal" target="_blank">quickly met with legal challenges</a>, which resulted in temporary blocks on the order's enforcement.</p><ul><li>Last month, the president asked the Supreme Court to overturn those lower court orders.</li></ul><h2>How many people would be affected by the order?</h2><p>In 2018, there were 4.4 million U.S.-born children with at least one parent who is an "unauthorized immigrant," per a <a href="https://www.migrationpolicy.org/sites/default/files/publications/mpi-unauthorized-immigrants-stablenumbers-changingorigins_final.pdf" target="_blank">Migration Policy Institute</a> analysis.</p><ul><li>Congress in 2022 defined unauthorized immigrants as "noncitizens who generally have entered the United States without inspection, overstayed a period of lawful admission, or violated the terms of their admission."</li><li>That figure represents most children living with parents who are unauthorized immigrants, 85%.</li></ul><h2>Has the number of children born to immigrants changed over the years?</h2><ul><li>In 2021, 21% of U.S. births were attributed to immigrant parents, the same share as in 2000, per child advocacy group <a href="https://datacenter.aecf.org/data/line/14-births-to-foreign-born-mothers?loc=1&amp;loct=1#1/any/false/2048,37,573,868,38,17,14,11/asc/any/272" target="_blank">Annie E. Casey Foundation</a>. This per­cent­age has fluctuated slightly over the years – rising to 25% in 2006 – but has since dropped back down to its pre­vi­ous level.</li></ul><h2>Could Trump erase birthright citizenship?</h2><p>Michael LeRoy, an immigration law expert and professor of labor and employment relations at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, <a href="https://www.axios.com/2024/12/10/trump-birthright-citizenship-executive-action" target="_blank">told</a> Axios' Avery Lotz last year that a Supreme Court ruling in Trump's favor would have far-reaching legal repercussions.</p><ul><li>While LeRoy says he can't predict how the Supreme Court would rule, striking down a "clear provision in an amendment based on an executive order" could have implications for other parts of the Constitution.</li><li>"The day might come when a president of a different political party issues an executive order to limit or repeal the Second Amendment," he said. "To be clear, that cannot happen."</li><li>He added, "You cannot have an executive order that repeals the right to bear arms, nor can you have an executive order that repeals the birthright citizenship clause."</li></ul>

Title: Scoop: U.S. presented Iran with nuclear deal proposal
Description: <p>The Trump administration gave Iran a proposal for a nuclear deal during the <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/11/iran-usa-nuclear-deal-talks" target="_blank">fourth round of negotiations</a> on Sunday, a U.S. official and two other sources with direct knowledge tell Axios. </p><p><strong>Why it matters:</strong> It was the first time since the nuclear talks started in early April that White House envoy <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/13/edan-alexander-release-secret-talks-bahbah" target="_blank">Steve Witkoff</a> presented a written proposal to the Iranians.</p><hr><ul><li>Iranian Foreign Minister Abbas Araghchi took the proposal back to Tehran for consultations with Supreme Leader Ali Khamenei, President Masoud Pezeshkian and other top officials.</li><li>President <a href="https://www.axios.com/politics-policy/donald-trump" target="_blank">Trump</a> said Tuesday <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/13/trump-gulf-trip-saudi-uae-qatar-partners" target="_blank">in Saudi Arabia</a> that he'd presented "an olive branch" to the Iranians but stressed "this is not an offer that will last forever. The time is right now for them to choose."</li></ul><p><strong>Behind the scenes:</strong> During the first round of talks, Araghchi gave Witkoff a written document with several Iranian proposals. Witkoff told Araghchi it was too soon for that and he wanted to build rapport first, a source with knowledge of that meeting said.</p><ul><li>During the third round of talks in late April, Araghchi gave Witkoff an updated document with Iranian ideas for a nuclear deal. This time, Witkoff took the document.</li><li>A U.S. team of experts studied it and sent the Iranians a list of questions and requests for clarification. The Iranians replied and added questions of their own, two sources said.</li><li>Meanwhile, Witkoff and his team prepared a U.S. proposal laying out the Trump administration's parameters for an Iranian civilian nuclear program and requirements for monitoring and verification, the sources said. </li></ul><p><strong>Between the lines:</strong> Witkoff and other officials have given contrasting answers as to whether and to what extent <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/04/trump-iran-nuclear-program-dismantled" target="_blank">Iran would be able to enrich uranium</a> under a deal, so it's significant that his team laid out the U.S. position in writing.</p><ul><li>A senior adviser to Khamenei <a href="https://www.nbcnews.com/news/us-news/iran-enriched-uranium-nuclear-deal-trump-rcna206833" target="_blank">told NBC News</a> Wednesday that Iran is prepared to sign a deal that allows it to enrich only to the lower levels needed for civilian uses and requires it to give up its stockpiles of highly enriched uranium — with inspectors verifying compliance — in exchange for lifting all sanctions.</li></ul><p><strong>Behind the scenes: </strong>Witkoff briefed the UN Security Council last Wednesday and described the U.S. proposal as "elegant" and "very big" but stressed more progress was needed in the negotiations, according to minutes of the meeting shared with Axios and confirmed by another source with knowledge.  </p><ul><li>Witkoff gave the proposal to Araghchi on Sunday in Oman, the sources said. A State Department official declined to comment. </li></ul><p><strong>State of play:</strong> Witkoff was encouraged by the results of the fourth round of talks, which took place a day before Trump set off on his Middle East trip. A fifth round hasn't been scheduled yet. </p><ul><li>"I hope you can help me with the Iran situation. It's a perilous situation, and we want to do the right thing," Trump told Qatari Emir Sheikh Tamim al-Thani when they met Wednesday.</li><li>Trump stressed he wants to avoid a war, "because things like that get started and they get out of control. I've seen it over and over again... we're not going to let that happen."</li></ul><p><strong>What's next:</strong> Senior diplomats from Iran and the E3 (France, U.K., Germany) are expected to meet in Istanbul of Friday for the first such meeting in two months. </p><ul><li>The three European powers have decided to trigger "snapback" sanctions under the 2015 nuclear deal if no new deal is reached by August, European diplomats say.   </li></ul>

Title: Biden officials "awed" by Trump's rule-breaking Middle East moves
Description: <p>President <a href="https://www.axios.com/politics-policy/donald-trump" target="_blank">Trump's</a> recent series of audacious foreign policy moves have astounded even some of his harshest critics.</p><p><strong>The big picture: </strong>Just in the Middle East and just in the past week, Trump has <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/14/trump-meets-syria-president-sharaa" target="_blank">met with a leader</a> the U.S. officially considers a terrorist, announced he'll lift all <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/13/trump-syria-al-sharaa-meeting-saudi" target="_blank">sanctions on Syria</a>, and cut a <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/06/houthi-ceasefire-trump-yemen-attacks" target="_blank">truce with the Houthis</a> plus <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/13/edan-alexander-release-secret-talks-bahbah" target="_blank">a hostage deal with Hamas</a>, both of which excluded Israel.</p><hr><p><strong>What they're saying:</strong> Biden administration veterans who spoke with Axios raised questions about Trump's motivations but grudgingly saluted his boldness.</p><ul><li>"Gosh, I wish I could work for an administration that could move that quickly," one admitted.</li><li>"He does all this, and it's kind of silence, it's met with a shrug," says Ned Price, a former senior State Department official under President Biden. "He has the ability to do things politically that previous presidents did not, because he has complete unquestioned authority over the Republican caucus."</li><li>"It's hard not to be simultaneously terrified at the thought of the damage he can cause with such power, and awed by his willingness to brazenly shatter so many harmful taboos," says Rob Malley, who held senior posts in three Democratic administrations, including handling <a href="https://www.axios.com/world/iran" target="_blank">Iran</a> talks under Presidents Obama and Biden.</li></ul><p><strong>Zoom out: </strong>On issue after issue, Trump is taking steps no recent president would have even considered.</p><ul><li>He abandoned the unified Western position to back <a href="https://www.axios.com/world/axios-explains-ukraine" target="_blank">Ukraine</a> "as long as it takes" by negotiating directly with Vladimir Putin and declaring that Kyiv will never get Crimea back and must cut a deal now.</li><li>He inserted himself directly in the recent Kashmir crisis, something past administrations have avoided so as not to antagonize India.</li><li>He endorsed <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/11/iran-usa-nuclear-deal-talks" target="_blank">direct talks with Iran</a> and shrugged off hawks at home and abroad who tied the Obama and Biden administrations in knots. It helps that many of them, like Israel's Benjamin Netanyahu, are loath to cross Trump.</li></ul><p><strong>Zoom in:</strong> On Syria, Trump's own State Department had espoused a policy similar to the Biden administration's before it — sanctions relief might be possible if militant-turned-statesman Ahmed al-Sharaa's government met a number of criteria, such as suppressing extremist groups.</p><ul><li>That meant that, somewhat perversely, the new administration was being strangled by sanctions imposed on the <a href="https://www.axios.com/2024/12/08/syria-damascus-assad-regime-collapse" target="_blank">dictator they toppled</a>. But it was also just the way these things work, until Trump decided it wasn't. </li><li>"It's so clearly the right decision," said Ben Rhodes, a national security aide to President Obama, on the "Pod Save the World" podcast. "I don't know why Joe Biden didn't do this."</li><li>"I don't like Trump's motivations for lots of things he does," Rhodes continued, "but one thing you will say is he's not tied to this constant fear of some bad faith right-wing attacks or stupid Blob-type, 'we don't do this, we must leverage the sanctions for blah blah blah.' No! Sometimes you just have to try something different."</li></ul><p><strong>Perhaps most shocking</strong> to veterans of previous administrations, Trump authorized direct talks with Hamas last month that Israeli officials only learned about through espionage (<a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/03/05/us-hamas-talks-gaza-war-israel" target="_blank">or by reading Axios</a>).</p><p><strong>Driving the news: </strong>This week, Trump's envoy-for-everything Steve Witkoff used backchannel talks to negotiate the release of Israeli-American hostage <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/12/hamas-releases-edan-alexander-last-living-american-hostage-in-gaza" target="_blank">Edan Alexander</a>, undercutting Israel's own negotiating tactics.</p><ul><li>"I suspect Witkoff doesn't have to look over his shoulder as much as others have. His predecessors have had to make sure the secretary of defense is on board and the secretary of the Treasury is on board, and the head of the CIA is on board. He just does it," the former Biden official said.</li></ul><p><strong>Flashback: </strong>The Biden administration considered but ultimately rejected opening direct talks with Hamas.</p><ul><li>"This is not about President Biden, this is about President Trump and the advantage that he has with Bibi [Netanyahu]," Price said. "If the Biden administration had done something like this, Bibi would have gone out guns blazing."</li><li>Now, even with the daylight between him and Trump growing more glaring, Netanyahu is keeping uncharacteristically quiet.</li></ul><p><strong>The flipside:</strong> Trump has also not pushed back hard as Netanyahu cut off all aid to Gaza, refused to budge in ceasefire negotiations, and announced a military <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/05/israel-gaza-destroy-trump-deal" target="_blank">operation to flatten and occupy the enclave</a>.</p><ul><li>Price argues that's the more nefarious side of Trump's a-la-carte "America First" approach. </li><li>"Unfortunately, we have a real-life experiment going on right now where we see exactly what happens when an administration abdicates that concerted pressure on the Israelis."</li></ul><p><strong>Between the lines: </strong>All the former Democratic officials who spoke to Axios questioned Trump's motives, even for policies they personally agreed with, noting that he's not just breaching norms to make peace, but also to sell cryptocurrency, expand his real estate portfolio or obtain a <a href="https://www.axios.com/2025/05/14/trump-qatar-air-force-one-presidential-gifts" target="_blank">$400 million jet</a>.</p><ul><li>The Syria announcement notably came at the urging of Saudi Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman and Turkish President Recep Tayyip Erdoğan, two leaders fluent in Trump-style transactionalism. </li></ul>

Title: Google dominates AI patent applications
Description: <div>Data: IFI Claims; Chart: Axios Visuals</div><p>Google has overtaken IBM to become the leader in generative AI-related patents and also leads in the emerging area of agentic AI, according to data from IFI Claims shared first with Axios.</p><p><strong>Why it matters:</strong> Patent filings, though they're not a direct proxy for innovation, indicate areas of keen research interest — and generative AI patent applications in the U.S. have risen by more than 50% in recent months.</p><hr><ul><li>"The surge in applications for AI related patents is a sign companies are actively seeking protection for their AI technologies, leading to an increase in grants as well," IFI Claims spokesperson Lily Iacurci said in a statement to Axios.</li></ul><p><strong>In the patents-for-agents U.S. rankings,</strong> Google and Nvidia top the list, followed by IBM, Intel and Microsoft, according to an <a href="https://www.ificlaims.com/news/view/evolution_of_ai_with_patents.htm" target="_blank">analysis</a> released Thursday.</p><ul><li>Globally, Google and Nvidia also led the agentic patents list, but three Chinese universities also make the top 10, highlighting China's place as the chief U.S. rival in the field. </li></ul><p><strong>In global rankings for generative AI,</strong> Google was also the leader — but six of the top 10 global spots were held by Chinese companies or universities. Microsoft was No. 3, with Nvidia and IBM also in the top 10.</p><ul><li>IFI Claims identified only a <a href="https://patents.google.com/patent/CN118246542A/en?oq=CN118246542A" target="_blank">single patent</a> tied to China's DeepSeek, one for a method of constructing training data.</li></ul><p><strong>In U.S. rankings for generative AI,</strong> Google and Microsoft topped the list of  U.S. patent applications, surpassing <a href="https://www.axios.com/2024/02/05/patent-applications-generative-ai-ibm-list" target="_blank">previous leader</a> IBM.</p><ul><li>Also in the top 10 were Nvidia, Capital One, Samsung, Adobe, Intel and Qualcomm.</li></ul><p><strong>Many of the same names </strong>cropped up in the list of overall AI-related U.S. patent applications, with Google in the top spot, followed by Microsoft, IBM, Samsung and Capital One. </p><ul><li>Globally, Google topped the list, followed by Huawei and Samsung.</li></ul><p><strong>Neither Meta nor OpenAI</strong> ranked in the top 10, though OpenAI has stepped up its patent efforts over the past year, IFI's analysis found.</p><ul><li>Meta has concentrated efforts on open source development, and OpenAI <a href="https://openai.com/approach-to-patents/" target="_blank">says</a> it intends to use its patents only "defensively."</li></ul><p><strong>By the numbers:</strong> Overall, the number of U.S. patent applications related to generative AI surged 56% last year, to 51,487. Granted patents in the U.S. also rose 32%.</p><ul><li>Generative AI made up 17% of AI-related U.S. patent applications, while agentic AI accounted for 7%. Globally, 23% of patent applications were related to generative AI and 5% to agentic AI.</li><li>The study covers the 14-month period from February 2024 to April 2025.</li></ul>

Title: Boeing lands biggest order in sale to Qatar Airways
Description: <p>Count Boeing as a big winner of the Trump administration's trade policy  —despite a sometimes frosty relationship with the president.</p><p><strong>Why it matters: </strong>It's a Title: After Two SpaceX Explosions, U.K. Officials Ask FAA to Change Starship Flight Plans
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/heather-vogell">Heather Vogell</a>                </p>
                                             

                                                                    
            
                <p>ProPublica is a nonprofit newsroom that investigates abuses of power. Sign up to receive <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/the-big-story?source=54G&amp;placement=top-note&amp;region=national">our biggest stories</a> as soon as they’re published.</p>

            

                



        
                        <p><strong>Update, May 15, 2025:</strong> This story has been updated to reflect the FAA’s announcement late Thursday that it had approved Starship 9 launch, which came after publication.</p>

        

    
      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.0">British officials told the U.S. they are concerned about the safety of SpaceX’s plans to fly its next Starship rocket over British territories in the Caribbean, where debris fell earlier this year after two of the company’s rockets exploded, according to documents reviewed by ProPublica.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.1">The worries from the U.K. government, detailed in a letter to a top American diplomat on Wednesday, follow the Federal Aviation Administration’s decision last week to grant SpaceX’s request for a fivefold increase in the number of Starship launches allowed this year, from five to 25. Growing the number of launches of the most powerful rocket ever built is <a href="https://www.propublica.org/article/elon-musk-spacex-doge-faa-ast-regulation-spaceflight-trump">a priority for SpaceX head Elon Musk</a>, who is also one of President Donald Trump’s closest advisers.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.2">Of particular concern to British officials is the public’s safety in the British Virgin Islands, Anguilla and the Turks and Caicos Islands — all of which could face debris risk from Starship 9. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.3">After the explosion in January, residents of the Turks and Caicos reported finding pieces of the rocket on beaches and roads. A car was also damaged in the Starship 7 accident. Seven weeks later, after receiving the FAA’s blessing to proceed, SpaceX launched Starship 8 from Boca Chica, Texas, but it too exploded after liftoff. Air traffic in the region was diverted, and burning streaks from the falling rocket were visible in the sky from the Bahamas and Florida’s coast.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.4">The British letter to a U.S. State Department official, Ambassador Lisa Kenna, asks the U.S. to consider changing the launch site or trajectory of Starship 9. If that isn’t possible, the request — from Stephen Doughty, the United Kingdom’s minister of state for Europe, North America and U.K. Overseas Territories — asks that agencies like the FAA consider altering the launch’s timing to minimize safety risks and the economic impact for the British territories.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.5">The letter also requests that the U.S. government provide the United Kingdom more information on increased safety measures that will be put in place before Starship 9 launches, and that British territories be given enough warning to communicate with the public about those measures.  </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.6">“We have been working closely with US Government partners regarding Starship Flight 9 to protect the safety of the UK Overseas Territories and to ensure appropriate measures are in place,” a  UK government spokesperson said Thursday in response to ProPublica’s questions about the letter.</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.0">The State Department did not respond to requests for comment.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.1">On Thursday afternoon, the FAA said it was “in close contact and collaboration with the United Kingdom and the Turks and Caicos Islands, as well as other regional partners, as we continue to evaluate SpaceX’s license modification request for its proposed Starship Flight 9 launch.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.2">Hours later, though, after this story originally published, the agency announced it had approved Starship 9’s launch, pending the completion of an investigation into the previous explosion. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.3">The agency also said it was expanding the "Aircraft Hazard Area" for the mission, stretching from the Gulf of Mexico to the Caribbean, potentially affecting 175 flights. That hazard area nearly encompasses the Turks and Caicos Islands in their entirety, <a href="https://www.faa.gov/media/94821">according to the FAA’s environmental assessment</a>. The agency said the changes were due to the prior Starship’s problems and because SpaceX plans to reuse a previously launched Super Heavy booster rocket — something it will be doing for the first time.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.4">Turks and Caicos’ Providenciales International Airport will need to close during the duration of the launch window, the assessment said. Airspace over a portion of The Bahamas will be closed, too.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.5">The FAA said the launch has been scheduled outside peak transit times to minimize disruptions.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.6">SpaceX did not respond to a request for comment. But the company has said it learns from its mistakes. “With a test like this, success comes from what we learn, and today’s flight will help us improve Starship’s reliability,” <a href="https://www.spacex.com/launches/mission/?missionId=starship-flight-8">the company said</a> after the Starship 8 accident. “We will conduct a thorough investigation, in coordination with the FAA, and implement corrective actions to make improvements on future Starship flight tests.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.7">Musk — who sees the uptick in launches as critical to the development of technology that could help land astronauts on the moon and ultimately Mars — has been less diplomatic. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.8">He downplayed the January explosion as “<a href="https://www.marketwatch.com/story/spacexs-starship-blew-up-elon-musk-says-its-barely-a-bump-in-the-road-c7672ae3">barely a bump in the road</a>” and seemed to brush off safety concerns, <a href="https://x.com/elonmusk/status/1880040599761596689?lang=en">posting a video of the flaming debris field</a> with the caption, “Success is uncertain, but entertainment is guaranteed!”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.9">SpaceX has not announced the date of the Starship 9 launch, but <a href="https://arstechnica.com/space/2025/05/spacex-test-fires-starship-for-an-all-important-next-flight/">news reports</a> have said it could happen as soon as May 21. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.10">The FAA’s Office of Commercial Space Transportation, which licenses launches and reentries, is undergoing a leadership shakeup. Three top executives, including the head of the office, announced in April that they were accepting voluntary separation offers.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.11">Musk has been leading efforts to shrink the federal government through the departures of thousands of federal workers. Critics say he has an inherent conflict of interest because his businesses are regulated by agencies such as the FAA and rely on their approvals. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.12">Musk said in a February interview that “<a href="https://www.npr.org/2025/02/21/nx-s1-5304123/elon-musks-role-in-government-raises-conflict-of-interest-issues">I’ll recuse myself if it is a conflict</a>.” White House spokesperson Harrison Fields said Thursday that “All administration officials will comply with conflict of interest requirements.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.13">Last year, <a href="https://www.faa.gov/newsroom/faa-proposes-633009-civil-penalties-against-spacex">the FAA proposed $633,000 in fines</a> against SpaceX for violations related to two previous launches. Musk, in turn, accused the FAA of engaging in “lawfare” and threatened to sue it for “regulatory overreach.” The administrative case remains open. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.14">The number of rocket launches has increased dramatically in recent years, leading pilots and academics to warn about a growing danger in the air for flights that have only minutes to get out of harm’s way when a mishap — as explosions and other failures are called in industry parlance — occurs. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.15">Researchers at the University of British Columbia found in a <a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-024-84001-2">study published in January</a> that the risk space objects pose to aircraft is rising. They said that the chance of an “uncontrolled reentry” from a rocket over a year is as high as 26% for some large, busy areas of airspace, such as those found in the northeastern U.S., in northern Europe or near major cities in the Asia-Pacific region.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.16">A large union for airplane pilots told FAA officials in January that the Starship 7 breakup “raises additional concerns about whether the FAA is providing adequate separation of space operations from airline flights,” according to a letter sent the day after the rocket exploded.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.17">“The ability of the FAA Air Traffic Control to respond in a timely fashion to an unanticipated rocket anomaly needs to be further evaluated,” said the letter from the Air Line Pilots Association, which represents 79,000 pilots at 42 U.S. and Canadian airlines. It asked that flight crews receive more information about high-risk areas before a launch so they can “make an informed and timely decision about their need to potentially reject flight plans that route their aircraft underneath space vehicle trajectories.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.18">In a response, the FAA said it would review its processes to see whether more can be done to prepare flight crews before a launch.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.19">Capt. Jason Ambrosi, the union’s president, said in a statement emailed to ProPublica that changes are necessary. “Any safety risk posed to commercial airline operations is unacceptable.”</p>
        
                                   

Title: Trump Administration Moves to Block the U.S. Travel of Mexican Politicians Who It Says Are Linked to the Drug Trade
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/tim-golden">Tim Golden</a>                </p>
                                             

                                                                    
            
                <p>ProPublica is a nonprofit newsroom that investigates abuses of power. Sign up to receive <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/the-big-story?source=54G&amp;placement=top-note&amp;region=national">our biggest stories</a> as soon as they’re published.</p>

            

                

      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.0">In what could be a significant escalation of U.S. pressure on Mexico, the Trump administration has begun to impose travel restrictions and other sanctions on prominent Mexican politicians whom it believes are linked to drug corruption, U.S. officials said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.1">So far, two Mexican political figures have acknowledged being banned from traveling to the United States. But U.S. officials said they expect more Mexicans to be targeted as the administration works through a list of several dozen political figures who have been identified by law enforcement and intelligence agencies as having ties to the drug trade.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.2">The list includes leaders of President Claudia Sheinbaum’s governing party, several state governors and political figures close to her predecessor, former President Andrés Manuel López Obrador, the U.S. officials said. They insisted on anonymity to discuss sensitive policy plans.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.3">The governor of the Mexican state of Baja California, Marina del Pilar Ávila, confirmed that she and her husband, a former congressman, were told their U.S. visas were revoked because of “a situation” involving her husband. “The fact that the State Department has cancelled my visa does not mean that I have committed something bad,” she said at a news conference on Monday.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="1.4">Sheinbaum said her government had asked U.S. officials to explain why Ávila was stripped of her visa but had been told that such matters are private and no further information was given.</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.0">The visa actions represent the latest political challenge for the new Mexican leader and her leftist National Regeneration Movement, known as Morena. Despite the country’s historic sensitivity to any hint of U.S. meddling, Sheinbaum has thus far bolstered her support at home by asserting Mexico’s sovereignty in discussions with President Donald Trump while also moving to meet his demands for action against the biggest traffickers.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="3.1">Mexican journalists reported that U.S. immigration officials also pulled the visa of another border-state governor, Américo Villarreal of Tamaulipas, an assertion that the governor’s spokesperson dismissed as “unconfirmed.” (Villarreal has been frequently accused of having ties to drug trafficking, which he has denied.) Last month, the mayor of that state’s second-largest city, Matamoros, was stopped from crossing the border into Brownsville, Texas, but he, too, insisted he had not been formally stripped of his visa.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="5.0">A State Department spokesperson declined to comment, noting that visa records are confidential under U.S. law.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="5.1">Three U.S. officials said the visa actions will likely in some cases be accompanied by Treasury Department sanctions that block individuals from conducting business with U.S. companies and freeze financial assets they have in the United States. Ávila said that she did not have any U.S. bank accounts and faced no such sanction.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="5.2">A spokesperson for the Treasury Department declined to comment on the sanctions plan.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            White House deputy chief of staff Stephen Miller
    
            (Tom Brenner/The Washington Post/Getty Images)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.0">When the administration imposed tariffs on Mexico in early March, it asserted that the country’s government had granted “safe havens for the cartels to engage in the manufacturing and transportation of dangerous narcotics, which collectively have led to the overdose deaths of hundreds of thousands of American victims.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.1">As part of what it has described as an all-out fight against fentanyl and other illegal drugs, the administration has designated some of the biggest Mexican trafficking gangs as terrorist organizations and explored the possibility of unilateral U.S. military actions against them, officials said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.2">The review of Mexican drug corruption was initiated by a small White House team that requested information from law enforcement agencies and the U.S. intelligence community about Mexican political, government and military figures with criminal ties.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.3">Officials said the group has been shaping the administration’s security policy with Mexico under the leadership of a deputy White House homeland security adviser, Anthony Salisbury. It is overseen by the deputy chief of staff, Stephen Miller.  </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.4">A spokesperson for the White House declined to comment in response to questions about the group’s role in initiating the travel sanctions.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="7.5">One official familiar with the team’s list said it overlaps with a file of about 35 Mexican officials that was compiled by Drug Enforcement Administration investigators in 2019, after López Obrador began shutting down Mexico’s cooperation with the United States in counterdrug programs.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="9.0">That earlier effort sought to identify Mexican government figures who could be criminally prosecuted for aiding drug traffickers. It led to the 2019 indictment in the U.S. of the country’s former security chief, Genaro García Luna, and his conviction on drug charges three years later in a New York federal court.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="9.1">The two former DEA officials in Mexico City who oversaw the compilation of the 2019 list, Terrance Cole and Matthew Donahue, also proposed that the State Department cancel the U.S. visas of some of the Mexican political figures named on it. Senior U.S. diplomats rejected that proposal.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="9.2">Cole is now awaiting Senate confirmation as the Trump administration’s new DEA administrator.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="9.3">Some current and former U.S. officials expressed concerns about the latest White House-led plan. They noted that the standard of proof required for both visa cancellations and Treasury sanctions is well below that of a criminal trial, which could encourage proponents of the measures to act on what might be less-than-solid information.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="9.4">Officials said the visa actions were being taken under Section 212 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, which stipulates that noncitizens can be found ineligible for entry to the United States if the government “knows or has reason to believe” that the foreigner “is or has been a knowing aider, abettor, assister, conspirator or colluder with others in the illicit trafficking” of illegal drugs. The law also allows the State Department to cancel the visas of relatives of a sanctioned official who may have benefited from their illicit gains.</p>
        
    
                            
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.0">One U.S. official said that while the visa withdrawals might send a powerful signal of the United States’ new willingness to challenge Mexican corruption, they could also stir new conflict between the two governments.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.1">“We should be using all the resources of the government to go after these people,” the official said, referring to corrupt Mexican officials. “But the bigger question is: Does this work with President Sheinbaum? Are you going to lose an opportunity now with a Mexican government that has been very compliant on the drug front?”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.2">A former Mexican ambassador to Washington, Arturo Sarukhaan, said further visa actions against prominent figures in Sheinbaum’s party would make it hard for her to continue claiming a “good” relationship with the United States despite Trump’s often openly confrontational tone.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.3">“But at the same time,” Sarukhaan added, “it gives her — a nationalistic president with a very chauvinistic party behind her — a perfect excuse to say that everything bad that’s happening in Mexico with the economy and everything else is because of U.S. imperialism.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.4">López Obrador, who came to power in 2018, had promised to fight corruption as never before. Instead, he presided over an administration that denied having any corruption problem in its own ranks even as journalists produced report after report that officials close to the president and even his own sons were engaged in profiteering and graft.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.5">Sheinbaum has struck a different tone. In a message to a Morena party congress on May 4, she warned the faithful about the dangers of cronyism, nepotism and corruption.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="11.6">“All members of Morena should conduct themselves with honesty, humility and simplicity,” she said. “There cannot be any collusion with crime — whether organized or white collar.”</p>

Title:  GDP增长-0.2%！日本经济一年来首次萎缩 
Description:  <p>日本经济在过去一年首次萎缩，关税、通胀对日本经济造成双重打击。</p>
<p>据日本政府周五发布的初步数据显示，<strong>日本一季度实际GDP环比下降0.2%，这是一年来首次经济收缩，弱于经济学家预测的0.1%萎缩，</strong>也与前一季度0.6%的增长形成鲜明对比，一季度日本经济按年率计算萎缩0.7%。</p>
<p>这一GDP数据发布之际，市场正担忧美国关税可能通过抑制出口和降低企业投资意愿，危及日本来之不易的经济复苏。</p>
<p data-type="paragraph">凯投宏观亚太区主管Marcel Thieliant表示：</p>
<blockquote>
<p data-type="paragraph">由于贸易战前夕经济已开始萎缩，日本央行可能要比我们预期等待更长时间才会恢复紧缩周期，日本央行7月份加息的难度越来越大。</p>
</blockquote>
<p data-type="paragraph">由于全球贸易前景的不确定性，日本央行最近将截至2026年3月财年的增长预期下调了一半。尽管日本央行仍在寻求进一步加息，但一些经济学家表示，如果贸易摩擦加深，央行今年可能无法采取任何行动。</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements">
<h2 id="629c58b3">出口疲软与通胀双重打击</h2>
</div>
<p><strong>第一季度数据显示，外部需求（出口减去进口）削减了0.8个百分点的增长，其中出口下降0.6%，进口则增长2.9%。</strong></p>
<p><strong>随着美国关税损害作为日本主要增长引擎之一的出口，持续的通胀也在损害另一个引擎：私人消费（约占GDP的一半）。周五发布的数据显示，第一季度私人支出环比持平。</strong></p>
<p>&#34;持续的价格上涨通过削弱消费者信心，对日本经济构成下行风险，&#34;经济大臣Ryosei Akazawa周五在新闻发布会上表示。</p>
<p>Akazawa表示，关税担忧尚未影响消费，但这位负责与美国进行贸易谈判的部长承诺，在最近的政府调查显示消费者情绪可能恶化后，将密切关注消费者情绪。</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements">
<h2 id="69ef8228">企业投资成为唯一亮点</h2>
</div>
<p>强劲的企业盈利推动公司增加投资并提供加薪。第一季度资本支出环比增长1.4%，这是数据中少有的亮点。</p>
<p>虽然较高的工资预计将有助于消费复苏，但食品通胀已经削弱了家庭的消费信心。</p>
<p>法国农业信贷银行经济学家Takuji Aida表示，日本央行需要确认日本经济在7-9月季度没有恶化，并在明年1月更新其预测，然后才能恢复加息。</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements">
<h2 id="fd232b30">技术性衰退可能性加大</h2>
</div>
<p><strong>包括Aida在内的一些经济学家预计，日本经济本季度可能再次萎缩，使该国陷入技术性衰退（定义为连续两个季度的收缩）。</strong></p>
<p>虽然第一季度GDP的第二次估计可能会向上修正，使经济摆脱衰退危险区，但穆迪日本和前沿市场经济主管Stefan Angrick表示，任何升级都不太可能过分积极地扭曲前景。</p>
<p>&#34;即将到来的数据不会改变经济正在挣扎的图景，&#34;他在一份报告中表示。</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements">
<h2 id="3d2a5542">美日贸易谈判成关键变量</h2>
</div>
<p>东京与华盛顿之间的谈判进展是另一个需要考虑的因素。</p>
<p>日本官员一直要求特朗普政府重新考虑关税，但能否达成协议尚不清楚。双方已同意在5月中旬或之后举行另一轮讨论。</p>
<p>到目前为止，&#34;2025年将是艰难的一年，因为关税痛苦和国内动力疲软威胁扼杀增长，&#34;Angrick表示。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">此外，日本政府和央行陷入两难境地，继续加息可能进一步抑制经济增长，但不加息又可能导致日元继续贬值和通胀问题加剧。无论哪种选择，都将使日本家庭和投资者在短期内面临更大风险。</p><p>本文来自华尔街见闻，欢迎下载APP查看更多</p>
