Title: How the Trump Administration Is Weakening the Enforcement of Fair Housing Laws
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/jesse-coburn">Jesse Coburn</a>                </p>
                                             

                                                                    
            
                <p>ProPublica is a nonprofit newsroom that investigates abuses of power. Sign up to receive <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/the-big-story?source=54G&amp;placement=top-note&amp;region=national">our biggest stories</a> as soon as they’re published.</p>

            

                

      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.0">Kennell Staten saw Walker Courts as his best path out of homelessness, he said. The complex had some of the only subsidized apartments he knew of in his adopted hometown of Jonesboro, Arkansas, so he applied to live there again and again. But while other people seemed to sail through the leasing process, his applications went nowhere. Staten thought he knew why: He is gay. The property manager had made her feelings about that clear to him, he said. “She said I was too flamboyant,” he remembered, “that it’s a whole bunch of older people staying there and they would feel uncomfortable seeing me coming outside with a dress or skirt on.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.1">So Staten filed a complaint with the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban Development in February. It was the type of complaint that HUD used to take seriously. The agency has devoted itself to rooting out prejudice in the housing market since the Fair Housing Act was signed into law in 1968, one week after the assassination of Martin Luther King Jr. And, following a <a href="https://www.fairhousingnc.org/newsletter/hud-announces-sexual-orientation-gender-identity-are-protected-by-federal-fair-housing-act/">2020 Supreme Court ruling</a> that declared that civil rights protections bar unequal treatment because of someone’s sexual orientation or gender identity, HUD considered it illegal to discriminate in housing on those grounds.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.2">Then Donald Trump became president once more. Two days after filing his complaint, Staten received a letter informing him that HUD did not view allegations like his as subject to federal law — a stark departure from its position just a month prior. The news gutted him. “I went through pure hell just to get turned away,” Staten said. (The property manager disputed Staten’s account and said he was rejected for fighting on the property, which Staten denied. The property owner declined to comment.) </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.3">Staten’s complaint is one of hundreds impacted by a major retreat in the federal government’s decadeslong fight against housing discrimination and segregation, according to interviews with 10 HUD officials. Those federal staffers, along with state officials, attorneys and advocates across the country, described a dismantling of federal fair housing enforcement, which has been slowed, constrained or halted at every step. The investigative process has been hobbled. The agency is withholding discrimination charges that HUD officials say should already have been issued. Those accused of housing discrimination appear newly emboldened not to cooperate with the agency. And at least 115 federal fair housing cases have been halted or closed entirely since Trump took office, with hundreds more cases in jeopardy, HUD officials estimate. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.4">These changes raise questions about the future of one of the enduring legacies of the civil rights movement, which advocates see as urgently needed today amid a historic housing shortage and rising complaints about housing discrimination. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.5">“It’ll give free rein to companies, to states, to governments to take advantage of people, to refuse to respect their rights, without fear of response from the government. They know that no one is watching, no one will hold them accountable, so they can just do what they want,” said Paul Osadebe, a HUD attorney and union steward who litigates fair housing cases. “The civil rights laws that people marched for and fought for and died for, that Congress passed and at least sensibly expects to be enforced, that’s just not happening right now. It’s not happening. And people are really being harmed by it.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.6">Asked to comment on the findings in this story, HUD spokesperson Kasey Lovett said in a statement: “HUD is committed to rooting out discrimination and upholding the Fair Housing Act. ProPublica continues to cherry pick examples to further an activist narrative rather than report the facts.” The White House did not respond to a request for comment.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            “They know that no one is watching, no one will hold them accountable, so they can just do what they want,” said Paul Osadebe, a HUD attorney and union steward who litigates fair housing cases.
    
            (Alyssa Schukar for ProPublica)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.0">For many victims of housing discrimination, HUD’s Office of Fair Housing and Equal Opportunity has long been the best path to winning justice. Recent investigations by the office and its state and local partners have led to millions of dollars in relief for victims and reforms from landlords, mortgage lenders and local governments. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.1">When a California city began requiring property owners to evict tenants if the county sheriff’s department said they had engaged in criminal activity — regardless of whether they were convicted — it was a HUD investigation that led to a nearly <a href="https://www.justice.gov/crt/case/united-states-v-city-hesperia-cd-cal">$1 million settlement</a> and a repeal of the ordinance. (The city did not admit liability.) The agency also secured a $300,000 settlement for a mother, daughter and the daughter’s boyfriend in Oklahoma who were allegedly <a href="https://www.hud.gov/sites/dfiles/FHEO/documents/Cushing%20Housing_OPMI%20LOF%2010.26.22.pdf">harassed and assaulted</a> by neighbors because the boyfriend was Black, to which the landlord responded by trying to evict the mother. (A representative for the property ownership company said company leadership has changed since the allegations.)</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.2">Such victories may be rare in the next four years.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.3">“We are being gutted right now,” said one agency official, who, like others, requested anonymity out of fear of retaliation. “And it feels like it’s not even the beginning.”</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.0">The Fair Housing Office’s staff of roughly 550 full-time employees is set to fall by more than a third through the administration’s federal worker buyout program, according to a HUD meeting recording obtained by ProPublica. Internal projections that have circulated widely among HUD staffers suggest far deeper cuts could follow.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.1">Those accused of housing discrimination seem to have taken notice. HUD officials described an increase in defendants ignoring correspondence from investigators or even copying Elon Musk’s Department of Government Efficiency in their communication with HUD, seemingly in hopes the cost-cutting department will take their side.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.2">“For them to face a consequence, they will need to be brought through a litigation process, which requires expenditure of litigation from the department, and they know that we don’t have those resources anymore,” one HUD official said. “They also feel emboldened that this administration will not consider the things that they are doing to be illegal.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.3">Some defendants have been more explicit about this. In one case, a midwestern city — which had allegedly allowed local politicians to block affordable housing in white neighborhoods — asked HUD officials if the agency still had the backing to pursue the case if the city walked away from the negotiating table, one official said. In another case, a public housing authority, also in the Midwest, rescinded a six-figure settlement it had offered two days prior, citing Trump’s newly issued <a href="https://www.whitehouse.gov/presidential-actions/2025/04/restoring-equality-of-opportunity-and-meritocracy/">executive order</a> attacking “disparate-impact liability.” The housing authority had allegedly favored white applicants and denied applicants with even modest criminal records. HUD spent years building the case; it crumbled in 48 hours. (HUD officials shared details on these and other cases on the condition that ProPublica not name the parties or locations, as the deliberations are private.)</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.4">Without the support of agency leadership, HUD is in a weaker negotiating position, dimming the prospects of major settlements or reforms. In another case involving a public housing authority, this one on the East Coast, HUD is considering settling for no monetary penalty — although it would not have accepted less than $1 million under the prior administration, officials said. HUD found the housing authority excluded disabled applicants and that some of its buildings had tenants who were disproportionately white (which the authority has denied).</p>


<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.5">When settlement negotiations collapse, HUD regularly issues “charges of discrimination,” akin to filing a lawsuit. Four months into Joe Biden’s presidency, <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20250110094756/https:/www.hud.gov/sites/dfiles/FHEO/documents/2021Charges.pdf">the agency had charged</a> at least eight cases and announced major steps in another four. In the second Trump presidency, HUD has not filed a single charge of housing discrimination, officials said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.6">It’s not for a lack of credible complaints, HUD officials say. There are dozens stuck in limbo at the agency’s Office of General Counsel, HUD officials estimated, including several where officials had conducted lengthy investigations and determined a civil rights law had been violated. One such complaint involves a New York woman who said she was sexually harassed for years by a maintenance worker in her building. The worker allegedly grabbed her breasts and told her that to receive repairs she would have to call him after hours — allegations that HUD officials found to be credible. But Trump appointees have not allowed them to file a charge, officials said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.7">Lovett, the HUD spokesperson, said that “the Department is preparing multiple charges that will be issued within the next week against individuals who we believe violated the Fair Housing Act.” She did not respond to a request for details about those charges.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.8">Many of the cases halted by HUD involve claims of housing discrimination because of someone’s sexual orientation or gender identity. Those appear to have been undermined by Trump’s “<a href="https://www.whitehouse.gov/presidential-actions/2025/01/defending-women-from-gender-ideology-extremism-and-restoring-biological-truth-to-the-federal-government/">defending women</a>” executive order, issued on his first day in office, which eliminated executive branch recognition of transgender people. <a href="https://www.whitehouse.gov/presidential-actions/2025/03/designating-english-as-the-official-language-of-the-united-states/">Another executive order</a> declaring English the country’s official language has paralyzed cases involving the requirement that housing providers who receive federal funds try to reach people with limited English proficiency. Other cases now in peril involve environmental justice, like disputes over the construction of pollution-emitting factories in poor, predominantly nonwhite neighborhoods. Race-based discrimination cases could be next on the chopping block, given the administration’s campaign against diversity, equity and inclusion efforts, some HUD officials fear.</p>


<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.9">Previously there were many channels through which the public could file housing discrimination complaints to HUD. In March, <a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/business/2025/04/06/fair-housing-enforcement-hud-cuts/">the agency shut down</a> all but one of them (with limited exceptions), citing staffing reductions. Now complaint hotlines and inboxes go unmonitored, with answering machines informing callers: “The number you reached is no longer in use.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.10">Investigations have been thwarted. Staffers can no longer travel to look for witnesses, as staff credit cards now have $1 spending limits. Agency attorneys must seek approval from a Trump appointee for basic tasks, such as issuing subpoenas, taking depositions, assisting with settlement discussions and even merely speaking to other attorneys in and outside government. As that approval seems to rarely come, investigations languish, HUD officials said. Even routine settlements now require approval from a political appointee, exacerbating the case backlog and delaying relief for victims, officials said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.11">The dysfunction has at times taken more mundane forms. For around two weeks in March, the Fair Housing Office’s work slowed to a crawl after DOGE canceled, without notice, a contract that had enabled staffers to quickly send certified mail to people involved in cases, according to officials and federal contracting data. It was a crucial resource — the office mails tens of thousands of documents each year, and regulations require some correspondence to be certified. Without the contract, staff had to spend their days stuffing envelopes themselves. The contract was worth only around $220,000. In recent years, HUD’s annual discretionary budget has topped $70 billion.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.12">Compliance reviews and discretionary investigations have also been affected. Typically that involves examining the policies and practices of developers, public housing authorities and other recipients of HUD funding to ensure that they abide by civil rights laws. Officials said such efforts have all but ceased, including an investigation into a housing authority that appeared to have a disproportionately low number of Latino tenants and applicants compared to the surrounding area. Larger, systemic investigations are similarly on ice.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.13">The apparent retreat in fair housing enforcement extends beyond HUD. At the Department of Justice, which prosecutes many fair housing cases, staffers received a draft of the housing section’s <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/us-news/2025/apr/18/justice-department-civil-rights-division-trump">new mission statement</a>, which omitted any mention of the Fair Housing Act. (The DOJ declined to comment.) At the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, Trump appointee Russ Vought has <a href="https://www.consumerfinance.gov/about-us/newsroom/cfpb-seeks-to-vacate-abusive-unjust-case-against-townstone/">sought to vacate</a> a settlement with a company called Townstone Financial, which CFPB alleged had effectively discouraged African Americans from applying for mortgages. The agency is now proposing to return the settlement funds to the company. “CFPB abused its power, used radical ‘equity’ arguments to tag Townstone as racist with zero evidence, and spent years persecuting and extorting them,” <a href="https://www.consumerfinance.gov/about-us/newsroom/cfpb-seeks-to-vacate-abusive-unjust-case-against-townstone/">Vought has said</a> to explain the decision. (CFPB did not respond to a request for comment. Townstone’s CEO said that he welcomed the move to vacate the settlement and that the prior allegations were meritless.)</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.14">The federal government’s fair housing efforts are supported by a broad ecosystem of local nonprofits. They, too, have been destabilized. In February, HUD and DOGE canceled 78 grants to local fair housing organizations, saying each one “no longer effectuates the program goals or agency priorities.” The funding represented a minuscule fraction of HUD’s budget but was essential to grant recipients. That includes groups like Housing Opportunities Made Equal of Greater Cincinnati, which was forced to pause investigations into racist mortgage lending practices and apartment buildings that may flout accessibility laws, according to Executive Director Elisabeth Risch. Four of the organizations filed a class-action lawsuit, arguing HUD and DOGE had no authority to withhold funding approved by Congress. The litigation is ongoing.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.15">Many states do not have their own substantial fair housing laws, leaving little recourse for housing discrimination victims in large swaths of the country if HUD’s retreat continues. “In the state of Missouri, HUD was it for housing protections,” said Kalila Jackson, an attorney in St. Louis. “It’s a terrifying situation.”</p>


<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.16">Fighting housing discrimination was once seen as so imperative that President Lyndon Johnson described the Fair Housing Act as a crowning achievement of the civil rights movement. “With this bill, the voice of justice speaks again,” he said <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=BN7tMJvlDNU&amp;t=44s">when signing the legislation</a>. “It proclaims that fair housing for all — all human beings who live in this country — is now a part of the American way of life. “</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.17">But advocates and HUD officials say that ambition never became a reality. “The fair housing laws were never fully implemented,” said Erin Kemple, a vice president at the National Fair Housing Alliance. “If you look at segregation throughout the country, it is still very high in most places.” And the Fair Housing Office has been chronically understaffed and underfunded by Republican and Democratic administrations alike. The office has long struggled to clear its docket. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.18">In recent years, segregation has been on the rise by some measures. One study found that most major metropolitan areas were <a href="https://belonging.berkeley.edu/roots-structural-racism">more segregated in 2019</a> than they had been in 1990. Another found that the Black homeownership rate is <a href="https://www.urban.org/sites/default/files/publication/103501/the-future-of-headship-and-homeownership.pdf">lower now than</a> it was at the passage of the Fair Housing Act. And <a href="https://nationalfairhousing.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/04/2024-Fair-Housing-Trends-Report-FINAL_07.2024.pdf">more housing discrimination complaints</a> were filed in 2023 than in any other year since the National Fair Housing Alliance began tracking the figures three decades ago.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.19">Some advocates fear that a four-year federal retreat from the issue could send the country sliding back toward the pre-civil rights era, when landlords and mortgage lenders could freely reject applicants because of their race, and when federal agencies, local governments and real estate brokers could maintain policies that perpetuated extreme levels of segregation.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.20">HUD officials interviewed by ProPublica echoed those concerns, foreseeing a growing national underclass of poor renters suffering discrimination with little hope of redress. They can always file lawsuits, but, for those at the bottom of the housing market, costly litigation is hardly an option. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.21">Even if today’s policies are undone by future administrations, there will be at least four years in which it may become easier for local zoning boards to block affordable housing, for mortgage lenders to retreat from nonwhite neighborhoods, and for developers to flout accessibility requirements in new buildings, HUD officials fear. The consequences of those changes could stretch far into the future. “Housing cycles are long,” one HUD official said. “This decimation will set us back for another several decades.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.22">April is Fair Housing Month, when HUD usually announces high-profile cases and holds events celebrating the Fair Housing Act. This April came and went without fanfare. HUD Secretary Scott Turner did release <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ymBjDdnHQCc">a two-minute video</a>, in which he vowed to “uphold the Fair Housing Act so every American has the opportunity to achieve the American dream of homeownership.” He added: “A more fair and free housing market is truly part of President Trump’s golden age of America.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.23">Beyond that, Turner has had little to say about housing discrimination or segregation, beyond <a href="https://www.politico.com/news/2025/02/26/trump-scraps-fair-housing-initiative-00206274">weakening a measure</a> known as Affirmatively Furthering Fair Housing. HUD even eliminated the Fair Housing Office’s old website. The URL now redirects to <a href="https://www.hud.gov/">HUD’s homepage</a>, which features a photo of a suburban cul-de-sac with a heavenly sunset behind it and a quote from Turner, a former NFL player and Baptist pastor.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.24">“God blessed us with this great nation,” it reads. “Together, we can increase self-sufficiency and empower Americans to climb the economic ladder toward a brighter future.”</p>
        
                                   

Title: The Trump Administration Leaned on African Countries. The Goal: Get Business for Elon Musk.
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/joshua-kaplan">Joshua Kaplan</a>, <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/brett-murphy">Brett Murphy</a>, <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/justin-elliott">Justin Elliott</a> and <a class="name" href="https://www.propublica.org/people/alex-mierjeski">Alex Mierjeski</a>                </p>
                                             

                                                                    
            
                <p>ProPublica is a nonprofit newsroom that investigates abuses of power. Sign up to receive <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/the-big-story?source=54G&amp;placement=top-note&amp;region=national">our biggest stories</a> as soon as they’re published.</p>

            

                

      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.0">In early February, Sharon Cromer, U.S. ambassador to Gambia, went to visit one of the country’s Cabinet ministers at his agency’s headquarters, above a partially abandoned strip mall off a dirt road. It had been two weeks since President Donald Trump took office, and Cromer had pressing business to discuss. She needed the minister to fall in line to help Elon Musk.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.1">Starlink, Musk’s satellite internet company, had spent months trying to secure regulatory approval to sell internet access in the impoverished West African country. As head of Gambia’s communications ministry, Lamin Jabbi oversees the government’s review of Starlink’s license application. Jabbi had been slow to sign off and the company had grown impatient. Now the top U.S. government official in Gambia was in Jabbi’s office to intervene.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.2">Musk’s Department of Government Efficiency loomed over the conversation. The administration had already begun freezing foreign aid projects, and early in the meeting, Cromer, a Biden appointee, said something that rattled Gambian officials in the room. She listed the ways that the U.S. was supporting the country, according to two people present and contemporaneous notes, noting that key initiatives — like one that funds a $25 million project to improve the electrical system — were currently under review. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.3">Jabbi’s top deputy, Hassan Jallow, told ProPublica he saw Cromer’s message as a veiled threat: If Starlink doesn’t get its license, the U.S. could cut off the desperately needed funds. “The implication was that they were connected,” Jallow said.</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.0">In recent months, senior State Department officials in both Washington and Gambia have coordinated with Starlink executives to coax, lobby and browbeat at least seven Gambian government ministers to help Musk, records and interviews show. One of those Cabinet officials told ProPublica his government is under “maximum pressure” to yield.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.1">In mid-March, Cromer escalated the campaign by writing to Gambia’s president with an “important request.” That day, a contentious D.C. meeting between Musk employees and Jabbi had ended in an impasse. She urged the president to circumvent Jabbi and “facilitate the necessary approvals for Starlink to commence operations,” according to a copy of the letter obtained by ProPublica. Jabbi told confidantes he felt the ambassador was trying to get him fired.</p>
        
    
                        

    
        

                            
                                                        
                                                        
                                

            Lamin Jabbi, first image, head of Gambia’s communications ministry, and Sharon Cromer, U.S. ambassador to Gambia
    
            (Via the Facebook pages of Gambia’s Ministry of Communications and Digital Economy, and the U.S. Embassy in Banjul, Gambia)
    
    
    

            
                    
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.0">The saga in Gambia is the starkest known example of the Trump administration wielding the U.S. government’s foreign policy apparatus to advance the business interests of Musk, a top Trump adviser and the world’s richest man.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.1">Since Trump’s inauguration, the State Department has intervened on behalf of Starlink in Gambia and at least four other developing nations, previously unreported records and interviews show.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.2">As the Trump administration has gutted foreign aid, U.S. diplomats have pressed governments to fast-track licenses for Starlink and arranged conversations between company employees and foreign leaders. In cables, U.S. officials have said that for their foreign counterparts, helping Starlink is a chance to prove their commitment to good relations with the U.S.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.3">In one country last month, the U.S. embassy bragged that Starlink’s license was approved despite concerns it wasn’t abiding by rules that its competitors had to follow.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.4">“If this was done by another country, we absolutely would call this corruption,” said Kristofer Harrison, who served as a high-level State Department official in the George W. Bush administration. “Because it is corruption.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.5">Helping U.S. businesses has long been part of the State Department’s mission, but former ambassadors said they sought to do this by making the positive case for the benefits of U.S. investment. When seeking deals for U.S. companies, they said they took care to avoid the appearance of conflicts or leaving the impression that punitive measures were on the table.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.6">Ten current and former State Department officials said the recent drive was an alarming departure from standard diplomatic practice — because of both the tactics used and the person who would benefit most from them. “I honestly didn’t think we were capable of doing this,” one official told ProPublica. “That is bad on every level.” Kenneth Fairfax, a retired career diplomat who served as U.S. ambassador to Kazakhstan, said the global push for Musk “could lead to the impression that the U.S. is engaging in a form of crony capitalism.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.7">The <a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/business/2025/05/07/elon-musk-starlink-trump-tariffs/">Washington Post previously reported</a> that Secretary of State Marco Rubio has instructed U.S. diplomats to help Starlink so it can beat its Chinese and Russian competitors. Multiple countries, including India, have sped up license approvals for Starlink to try to build goodwill in tariff negotiations with the Trump administration, the Post reported.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.8">ProPublica’s reporting provides a detailed picture of what that push has looked like in practice. After Gambia’s ambassador to the U.S. declined an interview about Starlink — a topic seen as highly sensitive given Musk’s position — ProPublica reporters traveled to the capital, Banjul, to piece together the events. This account is based on internal State Department documents and interviews with dozens of current and former officials from both countries, most of whom requested anonymity for fear of retaliation.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.9">In response to detailed questions, the State Department issued a statement celebrating Starlink. “Starlink is an America-made product that has been a game changer in helping remote areas around the world gain internet connectivity,” a spokesperson wrote. “Any patriotic American should want to see an American company’s success on the global stage, especially over compromised Chinese competitors.” Cromer and Starlink did not respond to requests for comment, nor did the office of the president of Gambia. Jabbi made Jallow available to discuss the situation.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.10">During the Biden administration, State Department officials worked with Starlink to help the company navigate bureaucracies abroad. But the agency’s approach appears to have become significantly more aggressive and expansive since Trump’s return to power, according to internal records and current and former government officials.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.11">Foreign leaders are acutely aware of Musk’s unprecedented position in the government, which he has used to help rewrite U.S. foreign policy. After Musk spent at least $288 million on the 2024 election, Trump gave the billionaire a powerful post in the White House. In mere months, Musk’s team has directed the firing of thousands of federal workers, canceled billions of dollars in programs and dismantled the U.S. Agency for International Development, which supported humanitarian projects around the world. African nations have been particularly hard-hit by the cuts.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.12">At the same time, Musk continues to run Starlink and the rest of his corporate empire. In past administrations, government ethics lawyers carefully vetted potential conflicts of interest. Though Trump once said that “we won’t let him get near” conflicts, the <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2025/02/11/us/politics/elon-musk-companies-conflicts.html">White House </a><a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2025/02/11/us/politics/elon-musk-companies-conflicts.html">has also suggested</a> Musk is responsible for policing himself. The billionaire has waved away criticisms of the arrangement, saying “<a href="https://www.npr.org/2025/02/21/nx-s1-5304123/elon-musks-role-in-government-raises-conflict-of-interest-issues">I’ll recuse myself</a>” if conflicts arise. “My companies are suffering because I’m in the government,” Musk said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.13">In a statement, the White House said Musk has nothing to do with deals involving Starlink and that every administration official follows ethical guidelines. “For the umpteenth time, President Trump will not tolerate any conflicts of interest,” spokesperson Harrison Fields said in an email.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.14">Executives at Starlink have seized the moment to expand. An April State Department cable to D.C. obtained by ProPublica quoted a Starlink employee describing the company’s approach to securing a license in Djibouti, a key U.S. ally in Africa that hosts an American military base: “We’re pushing from the top and the bottom to ram this through.”</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            The headquarters of Gambia’s Ministry of Communications and Digital Economy, a Cabinet agency headed by Lamin Jabbi
    
            (Brett Murphy/ProPublica)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.0">Musk entered the White House at a pivotal moment for Starlink. When the service launched in 2020, it had a novel approach to internet access. Rather than relying on underground cables or cell towers like traditional telecom companies, Starlink uses low-orbiting satellites that let it provide fast internet in places its competitors had struggled to reach. Expectations for the startup were sky high. Bullish Morgan Stanley analysts predicted that by 2040, Starlink would have up to 364 million subscribers worldwide — more than the current population of the U.S. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.1">Starlink quickly became a central pillar of Musk’s fortune. His stake in Starlink’s parent company, SpaceX, is estimated to be worth about $150 billion of his roughly $400 billion net worth. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.2">Although the company says its user base has grown to over 5 million people, it remains a bit player compared to the largest internet providers. And the satellite internet market is set to become more competitive as well-funded companies launch services modeled on Starlink. Jeff Bezos’ Project Kuiper, a unit of Amazon, has said it expects to start serving customers later this year. Satellite upstarts headquartered in Europe and China aren’t far behind either.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.3">“They want to get as far and as fast as they can before Amazon Kuiper gets online,” said Chris Quilty, a veteran space industry analyst. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.4">In internal cables, State Department officials have said they are eager to help Musk get ahead of foreign satellite companies. Securing licenses in the next 18 months is critical for Starlink due to the growing competition, one cable said last month. Senior diplomats have written that they hope to give Musk’s company a “first-mover advantage.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.5">Africa represents a lucrative prize. Much of the continent lacks reliable internet. Success in Africa could mean dominating a market with the <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2023/10/28/world/africa/africa-youth-population.html">fastest-growing population</a> on earth.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            A technician mounts a Starlink satellite dish on a house in Niamey, Niger.
    
            (Boureima Hama/AFP/Getty Images)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.0">As of last November, Starlink had reportedly launched in 15 of Africa’s 54 countries, but it was beginning to spark a backlash. Last year, Cameroon and Namibia cracked down on Musk’s company for allegedly operating in their countries illegally. In South Africa — where Starlink has so far failed to get a license — Musk exacerbated tensions by publicly accusing the government of anti-white racism. Since Trump won the election, at least five African countries have granted licenses to Starlink: the Democratic Republic of Congo, Somalia, Guinea-Bissau, Lesotho and Chad.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.1">Now Musk’s campaign of cuts has given him leverage inside the State Department. A Trump administration memo that <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2025/04/15/us/politics/state-dept-expands-plans-for-closing-embassies-and-consulates.html">leaked to the press</a> last month proposed closing six embassies in Africa. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.2">The Gambian embassy was on the list of proposed cuts.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.3">An 8-year-old democracy, Gambia’s 2.7 million residents live on a sliver of land once used as a hub in the transatlantic slave trade. For two decades until 2017, the nation was ruled by a despot who had his opponents assassinated and plundered public funds to buy himself luxuries like a Rolls-Royce collection and a private zoo. When the dictator was ousted, the economy was in tatters. Today Gambia is one of the poorest countries in the world, with about half the country living on less than $4 a day.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.4">In this fragile environment, the telecom industry that Jabbi oversees is vitally important to Gambian authorities. According to the government, the sector provides at least 20% of the country’s tax revenue. Ads for the country’s multiple internet providers are ubiquitous, painted onto dozens of public works — parks, police booths, schools. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="10.5">It’s unclear why Starlink’s efforts in Gambia, a tiny market, have been so intense.</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            Banjul, the capital of Gambia, during New Year’s celebrations
    
            (Muhamadou Bittaye/AFP/Getty Images)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.0">Cromer’s efforts on behalf of the company started under the Biden administration, as she documented last December in a cable sent back to Washington. Last spring, Starlink began the process of securing necessary approvals from a local utilities regulator and the Gambian communications agency. The utilities regulator wanted Starlink to pay an $85,000 license fee, which the company felt was too expensive. Cromer spoke to local officials, who then “pressured” the regulator to remove “this unnecessary barrier to entry,” the ambassador wrote.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.1">Gambian supporters of Starlink felt that its product would be a boon for consumers and for economic growth in the country, where internet service remains unreliable and slow. “The ripple effects could be extraordinary,” Cromer said in the December cable, contending it could enable telehealth and improve education. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.2">Opponents argued that local internet providers were one of Gambia’s few stable sources of jobs and infrastructure investments. If Starlink killed off its competition and then jacked up its prices — in Nigeria, the company announced last year it would suddenly double its fees — authorities could have little leverage to manage the fallout. When Musk refused to turn on Starlink in part of Ukraine during the war there, it heightened concerns about handing control of internet access to the mercurial billionaire, industry analysts said. One Musk tweet about foreign regulators’ ability to police his company caught the attention of Gambian critics: “They can shake their fist at the sky,” Musk said in 2021.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.3">The ultimate authority for granting Starlink a license lies with Jabbi, an attorney who spent years in the local telecom sector. Gambian telecom companies that don’t want competition from Musk see Jabbi as an ally. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.4">Jallow, Jabbi’s top deputy, told ProPublica that the ministry is not opposed to Starlink operating in Gambia. But he said Jabbi is doing due diligence to ensure laws and regulations are being followed before opening up the country to a consequential change. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.5">After Trump’s inauguration, Jabbi’s position pitted him against not only Starlink but also the U.S. government. In the weeks after the February meeting where Cromer reminded Jabbi about the tenuous state of American funding to his country, the ambassador told other diplomats that getting Starlink approved was a high priority, according to a Western official familiar with her comments.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.6">The stance surprised some of Cromer’s peers. Cromer had spent her career at USAID before President Joe Biden appointed her as ambassador. Her tenure in Gambia often focused on human rights and democracy building.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.7">In March, when Jabbi and Jallow traveled to D.C. to attend a World Bank summit, the State Department helped arrange a series of meetings for them. The first, on March 19, was with Starlink representatives including Ben MacWilliams, a former U.S. diplomat who leads the company’s expansion efforts in Africa. The second was with U.S. government officials at the State Department’s headquarters.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.8">The meeting with the company quickly became contentious. Huddled in a conference room at the World Bank, MacWilliams accused Jabbi of standing in the way of his nation’s progress and harming ordinary Gambians, according to Jallow, who was in the meeting, and four others briefed on the event. “We want our license now,” Jallow recalled MacWilliams saying. “Why are you delaying it?” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.9">The conversation ended in a stalemate. In the hours that followed, Starlink and the U.S. government’s campaign intensified in a way that underscored the degree of coordination between the two parties. The company told Jabbi it would cancel his scheduled D.C. meeting with State Department officials because “there was no more need,” Jallow said. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.10">The State Department meeting never happened. Instead, 4,000 miles away in Gambia’s capital, Cromer would try an even more aggressive approach.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.11">That same day, Cromer had already met with Gambia’s equivalent of a commerce secretary to lobby him to help pave the way for Starlink. Then she was informed about the disappointing meeting Starlink had had in D.C., according to State Department records. By day’s end, Cromer had sent a letter to the nation’s president.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.12">“I am writing to seek your support to allow Starlink to operate in The Gambia,” the letter opened. Over three pages, the ambassador described her concerns about Jabbi’s agency and listed the ways that Gambians could benefit from Starlink. She also said the company had satisfied conditions set by Jabbi’s predecessor.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.13">“I respectfully urge you to facilitate the necessary approvals for Starlink to commence operations in The Gambia,” Cromer concluded. “I look forward to your favorable response.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.14">In the weeks since, Jabbi has refused to budge. The U.S. government’s efforts have continued. In late April, Gambia’s attorney general met in D.C. with senior State Department officials, according to a person familiar with the matter, where they again discussed the Starlink issue. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.15">Diplomats were troubled by how the pressure campaign could hurt America’s image overseas. “This is not Iran or a rogue African state run by a dictator — this is a democracy, a natural ally,” said another senior Western diplomat in the region, noting that Gambia is “a prime partner of the West” in United Nations votes. “You beat up the smallest and the best boy in the class.”</p>


<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.16">Gambia is not the only country being leaned on. Since Trump took office, embassies around the world have sent a flurry of cables to D.C. documenting their meetings with Starlink executives and their efforts to cajole developing countries into helping Musk’s business. The cables all describe a problem similar to what happened in Gambia: The company has struggled to win a license from local regulators. In some countries, ambassadors reported, their work appears to be yielding results. (The embassies and their host countries did not respond to requests for comment.)</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.17">The U.S. embassy in Cameroon wrote that the country could prove its commitment to Trump’s agenda by letting Starlink expand its presence there. In the same missive, embassy officials discussed the impact of U.S. aid cuts and deportations and cited a humanitarian official who was reckoning with America’s shifting foreign policy: “They may not be happy with what they see, but they are trying to adapt as best they can.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.18">In Lesotho, where embassy officials had spent weeks trying to help Starlink get a license, the company finalized a deal after Trump imposed 50% tariffs on the tiny landlocked country. Lesotho officials told embassy staff they hoped the license would help in their urgent push to reduce the levies, <a href="https://www.motherjones.com/politics/2025/04/trump-tariffs-lesotho-state-department-starlink-deportees/">according to Mother Jones</a>. A major multinational company complained that Starlink was getting preferential treatment, embassy documents obtained by ProPublica show, since Musk’s firm had been exempted from requirements its competitors still had to follow. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.19">In cables sent from the U.S. embassy in Djibouti this spring, State Department officials recounted their meetings with the company and pledged to continue working with “Starlink in identifying government officials and facilitating discussions.” </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.20">In Bangladesh, U.S. diplomats pressed Starlink’s case “early and often” with local officials, partnered with Starlink to “build an educational strategy” for their counterparts and helped arrange a conversation between Musk and the nation’s head of state, according to a recent cable. The embassy’s work started under Biden but bore fruit only after Trump took office.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="12.21">Their efforts resulted <a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2025/04/30/elon-musk-starlink-bangladesh-tariffs/">in Bangladesh</a> approving Starlink’s request to do business in the country, the top U.S. diplomat there said last month, a sign-off that Musk’s company had sought for years.</p>
        
             

                                    
        
                        <p>Do you have information about Elon Musk’s businesses or the Trump administration? Josh Kaplan can be reached by email at <a href="mailto:joshua.kaplan@propublica.org">joshua.kaplan@propublica.org</a> and by Signal or WhatsApp at 734-834-9383. Brett Murphy can be reached at 508-523-5195 or by email at <a href="mailto:brett.murphy@propublica.org">brett.murphy@propublica.org</a>.</p>

        

    


                                    
        
                        <p><a href="https://www.propublica.org/people/anna-maria-barry-jester">Anna Maria Barry-Jester</a> contributed reporting.</p>

        

    
                                         

Title: Connecticut Towing Companies Use Belongings Left in Cars as Leverage to Collect Fees, Drivers Say
Description: 
                <p class="byline">
                by <a class="name" href="https://ctmirror.org/author/gmonk/">Ginny Monk</a> and <a class="name" href="https://ctmirror.org/author/daltimari/">Dave Altimari</a>, <a href="https://ctmirror.org/">The Connecticut Mirror</a>                </p>
                                             

        
                        <p>This article was produced for ProPublica’s Local Reporting Network in partnership with <a href="https://ctmirror.org/">The Connecticut Mirror</a>. <a href="https://www.propublica.org/newsletters/dispatches">Sign up for Dispatches</a> to get our stories in your inbox every week.</p>

        

    
      
  



                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.0">Gary Hudson excitedly planned a fishing trip with his 4-year-old son and purchased a kids fishing pole in late 2019. He tossed it in the trunk of his Ford Taurus and parked on the street outside his Hartford, Connecticut, home.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.1">Within hours, his car was hauled away by a tow truck. Hudson couldn’t afford to pay the more than $300 in towing and storage fees and asked if he could at least get into the car to collect his belongings — the fishing pole and the safety vest and handcuffs he needed to work nights as a security guard. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.2">He said he offered to pay $20 but that Whitey’s, a Hartford towing company, told him he had to pay the full amount. “They would not budge, period,” Hudson said. “So I can’t get my work equipment, and you expect me to make money to pay you?” When Hudson couldn’t afford to retrieve the car, he said, Whitey’s sold it, and he lost his belongings. Whitey’s has since closed, and its owner has died.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.3">The Connecticut Mirror and ProPublica have heard repeatedly from people with similar stories. Inside their vehicles, they had work equipment, child car seats or personal mementos, and towing companies refused to give them back.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="2.4">Connecticut Department of Motor Vehicles regulations say that vehicle owners can retrieve “personal property which is essential to the health or welfare of any person.” But that gives towing companies wide latitude in how they interpret the rule, and several people whose cars were towed said the companies used their belongings as leverage to get them to pay towing and storage fees.</p>
        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.0">Past reporting by CT Mirror and ProPublica showed <a href="https://www.propublica.org/series/on-the-hook">how Connecticut’s laws have come to favor tow companies</a> at the expense of vehicle owners. Connecticut has <a href="https://www.propublica.org/article/connecticut-dmv-tow-companies-car-sales">one of the shortest windows</a> in the country between when a car is towed and when tow companies can consider it abandoned and start the process of selling it — companies have to wait just 15 days for vehicles worth less than $1,500. People with low incomes have been particularly impacted by these laws, the news organizations found.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.1">Some nearby states, like Rhode Island, have no law on the books about getting possessions from towed cars. But in those that do, the list of items owners must be allowed to retrieve is often broader than Connecticut’s. Maine allows people to retrieve clothing, car seats, medications and mail. In New York, people can retrieve anything from the vehicle. A bill in the Massachusetts legislature would let its drivers do the same.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.2">In an interview last year, Michelle Givens, the Connecticut DMV’s assistant legal director, said she couldn’t say whether work equipment qualified as essential to health or welfare.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="4.3">“It’s broad,” Givens said. “I can’t answer that and sit here and say, ‘Yes, that will qualify.’”</p>
        
    
                    

    
        <p>So I can’t get my work equipment, and you expect me to make money to pay you?</p>

    
        
        —Gary Hudson, a security guard who was not allowed to get his belongings out of his towed car

    
    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.0">DMV Commissioner Tony Guerrera said he thought car owners should file a complaint with the agency if they weren’t able to get their belongings out. The complaint process can take weeks, however, which is often longer than the period before a towing company is allowed to sell a car.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.1">Timothy Vibert, president of the industry association Towing &amp; Recovery Professionals of Connecticut, said people can generally retrieve medicine or tools, but he said that part of the law shouldn’t apply if people wait months to get them. He added that when people don’t pay the towing fees, it makes towers reluctant to return their belongings.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.2">“If somebody owed you $800 and they called up and said they wanted to get something out of their car,” he asked, “it’s OK for them to waltz down here and take their things and then leave you with an $800 bill?”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.3">Other towers say they are more lenient. Sal Sena, owner of Sena Brothers and Cross Country Automotive in Hartford, said if someone has keys to the vehicle or can prove it’s theirs, he lets them get stuff out of it regardless if they pay the fees.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.4">“I don’t care if you take stuff out, but I just want to make sure you’re not putting my ass in a situation where I’m gonna get in trouble,” Sena said. “You got the key? Then take what you want out of the car because then I can justify it.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.5">Connecticut lawmakers are looking to change the state’s towing laws. House Bill 7162, which was voted out of committee in March, would overhaul the law and allow owners to retrieve “any personal property” from a towed motor vehicle. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.6">The bill “makes a strong effort to identify and correct abusive practices in the towing industry that have had a serious and detrimental effect on motor vehicle owners,” legal aid attorney Rafie Podolsky said in public testimony.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.7">Tow company employees and owners have objected to the bill, saying it would make it harder for them to tow vehicles that are parked illegally or unsafely and that towers didn’t have enough involvement in crafting the legislation.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.8">Transportation Committee co-chair Sen. Christine Cohen, D-Guilford, said during a March meeting that the importance of the issue hit home for her because of “the number of folks” who have told her they got towed and weren’t allowed to retrieve belongings from their vehicles.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.9">“The people should certainly be made aware of their rights with respect to towed vehicles,” she said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.10">Hudson, who had planned the fishing trip, had to save up to replace his holster, mace and safety equipment for the security job, which he estimated cost him about $1,000. He canceled the fishing trip and said he failed his son “by breaking a promise.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.11">“It really, really hurt,” Hudson said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.12">Hudson is one of several people who told the news organizations they lost things they needed for work — tools, chef’s knives, even the draft of a movie script. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.13">Paul Boudreau, a carpenter and mechanic in Hamden, said he lost his entire carpentry tool set worth more than $1,500 when his Chevrolet Blazer was towed from his apartment complex in April 2021. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.14">The vehicle wasn’t registered because it couldn’t pass an emissions test, and his mechanic was waiting on a part that was hard to get during the supply chain crisis following the COVID-19 lockdown. The apartment complex’s management gave him more time to get it registered, he said, so he was surprised when he looked out his window and saw a tow truck hooking up his vehicle. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.15">He said MyHoopty.com, a towing company in Watertown, told him it would cost more than $300 to get it back. With his wife recovering from cancer, his carpentry work scarce because of the pandemic and “not a penny in cash,” Boudreau realized he couldn’t afford to retrieve his car. </p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.16">Still, he asked multiple times to retrieve his tools and was denied, he told the DMV in a complaint, which included an itemized list of tools. But MyHoopty owner Michael Festa said in an interview, “At no point did anyone contact us or attempt to come down and retrieve any personal belongings that may have been in the vehicle.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.17">The Connecticut DMV found that MyHoopty committed no violations related to the tow but did not address the items Boudreau said were in the vehicle.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.18">“Anybody we talked to was like, ‘There’s nothing we can do,’” Boudreau said in an interview.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.19">After 18 days, MyHoopty submitted a form to sell the Blazer.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="6.20">The tows at his apartment complex led Boudreau to become a tenant union organizer. He said state legislators always tell him that when it comes to landlords, their “property is sacred.”</p>
        
    
                    

    
        

                        
                    
                

            Paul Boudreau, center, speaks at a Connecticut Tenants Union rally at the state Capitol last year. His experience having his car towed led him to become a tenant organizer.
    
            (Shahrzad Rasekh/CT Mirror)
    
    
    

    

        
    
                    
<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.0">“Why isn’t our property sacred? Why isn’t our car sacred?” Boudreau asked about tenants. “Wealthy people’s property is always sacred, but poor people’s property doesn’t mean a thing.”</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.1">Other drivers lost belongings that held sentimental value — photographs, a sewing project, a prayer card from their father’s funeral.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.2">When Brandon Joyner’s Nissan Maxima was towed from the front of his Bridgeport home in 2017, he lost photos of his mother and aunt that had never been digitized, which he’d traveled with since he got his license as a teenager. He also had shoes, clothing and a car seat for his nieces and nephews in the vehicle, he said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.3">The car was towed because Joyner owed motor vehicle taxes on it. After a couple of weeks of saving, he paid the taxes. But when he asked for his car, he said he was told it had been sold.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.4">“Everything was just gone,” he said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.5">It took him months to afford a new vehicle, in part because he was still paying down the old loan from the bank. When he told them he no longer had the vehicle and didn’t want to pay on it, it damaged his credit score, making it harder to get a loan for a new car, he said.</p>

<p data-pp-blocktype="copy" data-pp-id="8.6">“It was hurtful, because there’s nothing you can really do,” Joyner said. “No matter how many people you talk to, you lose things, and it’s nobody’s fault, nobody cares.”</p>
        
    
                    
    
        <p>
            <strong><a href="https://www.propublica.org/getinvolved/car-towing-connecticut-help-propublica-investigate">Has Your Car Been Towed in Connecticut? Share Your Story and Help Us Investigate.</a></strong>
        </p>

    
        
             

                                    
        
                        <p><a href="https://www.propublica.org/people/asia-fields">Asia Fields</a> contributed reporting.</p>

Title:  砸钱买币与特朗普“私聊”，25个名额都有谁？孙宇晨买最多！ 
Description:  <p style="text-align: left;">购买特朗普币，就能与获得与总统“面对面”的机会，持有量前25名的名单已经公布，排名第一的是孙宇晨！</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">特朗普币官方网站近日宣布，5月22日特朗普币晚宴入场的名单已经出炉，加密情报公司Inca Digital称，为了赢得与特朗普共进晚餐的机会，特朗普币买家总共花费了约1.48亿美元。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">特朗普币官网显示了前220名特朗普币持有者的排行榜。排行榜上名列第一的是一个名为“SUN”的钱包，持有价值约1790万美元的特朗普币。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">据<span style="color: #1482f0;"><a style="color: #1482f0;" href="https://wallstreetcn.com/articles/3745798" target="_blank" rel="noopener">华尔街见闻此前提及</a></span>，特朗普币官网4月宣布，5月22日在特朗普私人高尔夫俱乐部邀请持币量前220名持有者共进晚餐，持有量前25名更是能够获得超级VIP待遇，包括参观白宫的机会。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">这一消息引发了特朗普币的疯狂抢购。自晚宴宣布以来，特朗普币价格上涨了近30%，不过仍远低于1月的峰值。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/f14e4130-fd18-4079-afb5-4da5966ffbc5.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="645" data-wscnw="811"/></p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">砸钱买特朗普币，孙宇晨买最多</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">在过去三周，“特朗普币”的价格波动剧烈，众多支持者和加密爱好者争相购买，以争取参与晚宴的资格。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">根据特朗普币官网排行榜，名列第一的是一个名为“SUN”的钱包。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">根据Lookonchain的数据，该钱包由波场币创始人、加密货币亿万富翁孙宇晨（Justin SUN）所有。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">据两家加密货币取证公司称，该账户属于一家名为HTX的海外交易所，亿万富翁、加密货币平台波场的创始人孙宇晨担任该交易所的全球顾问。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">排名第二的是新加坡加密货币初创公司MemeCore，持有价值约1740万美元的特朗普币，是第二大持有者。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">该公司正在开发一个面向迷因币创建者的平台，首席业务发展官Cherry Hsu表示，正寻求与美国迷因币社区建立桥梁。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">另据报道，澳大利亚企业家Kain Warwick也在排行榜上，他表示希望能在晚宴上和特朗普或他的二儿子Eric Trump聊几分钟。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">一位来自纽约的加密货币爱好者Vincent Deriu通过购买约11.6万美元的特朗普币赢得了参加晚宴的资格。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">他表示，即将举行的晚宴是一种比传统的大额资金筹款活动更民主的方式，这可能是唯一允许接触总统的机会。</p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">政府监督组织谴责晚宴</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">据报道，大量外国投资者占据VIP席位，伦理观察组织警告，这可能违反联邦规定和宪法的授权条款。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">据区块链分析公司Inca Digital的数据，在有资格参加接待会的25个钱包中，至少有17个收到了来自美国人无法使用的非美国加密货币交易所的资金。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">这表明VIP接待会的许多与会者可能是外国人，加剧了外界对特朗普家族商业利益与外国势力之间潜在利益冲突的担忧。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">&#34;这是一场伦理噩梦，&#34;奥巴马政府前白宫特别伦理和政府改革顾问Norm Eisen表示。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">据报道，在特朗普第二个任期的四个月里，他和他的家人频频利用总统职位为家族产业谋利益。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">除了迷因币外，他们在加密货币领域的涉足还包括一家比特币挖矿公司和World Liberty Financial，这是由特朗普的儿子们推广的风险投资项目。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><span style="color: #1482f0;"><a style="color: #1482f0;" href="https://wallstreetcn.com/articles/3746514" target="_blank" rel="noopener">华尔街见闻此前提及</a></span>，特朗普次子Eric Trump早前宣布，由阿布扎比政府支持的一家风险投资公司将使用World Liberty提供的数字货币进行20亿美元的投资。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">批评人士表示，鉴于总统已经减少了加密货币监管，这些都构成了利益冲突。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">康涅狄格州民主党参议员Richard Blumenthal称，这将是美国历史上最令人发指的利用职权谋取私利的行为之一。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">白宫方面表示，这场由特朗普在其社交平台上推广的拍卖活动不会影响总统的政策决策。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">白宫新闻秘书罗琳·莱维特此前对记者表示，特朗普在其加密货币相关活动中“完全遵守所有利益冲突法律”。</p><p>本文来自华尔街见闻，欢迎下载APP查看更多</p> 

Title:  Bill Ackman一季度：增持Uber，赶在关税前清仓了耐克 
Description:  <p style="text-align: left;">根据周四提交给美国证券交易委员会（SEC）的监管文件，亿万富翁、对冲基金大佬Bill Ackman的潘兴广场资本管理公司（Pershing Square）一季度大幅增持Uber，使其成为投资组合第一大持仓股，还增持了Brookfield和谷歌A类股，清仓耐克，减持希尔顿和谷歌C类股。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">值得注意的是，谷歌A类股和C类股不同地方在于，A类股具有投票权；C类股没有投票权。此外，从整个投资组合来看，截至2025年第一季度末，Ackman的投资组合包括11只股票，主要集中在消费循环、科技、金融服务、通信服务、房地产和工业六个行业。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/00c5892f-d8be-4af5-8d2e-c0bd59a3764d.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="852" data-wscnw="1173"/></p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">投资组合重大调整：Uber登顶，耐克出局</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">根据最新提交的13F文件显示，Bill Ackman的Pershing Square在2025年第一季度进行了重大投资组合调整。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">文件显示，Ackman一季度增持超过3030万股Uber Technologies的持仓，总价值达22.1亿美元，这使得Uber一跃成为其投资组合的最大持仓，占比高达18.5%。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/07285d6e-3327-43e2-8ff8-45e8d8dc8312.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="512" data-wscnw="1173"/></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">与此同时，Ackman完全清仓了此前持有的1876.9万股耐克股票，值得注意的是，这是赶在特朗普所谓“对等关税”前采取行动。</p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">重要增持：Brookfield和谷歌A类股成为焦点</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">除了Uber之外，Ackman还增加了全球最大的另类资管巨头之一的Brookfield和谷歌A的持仓。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">将Brookfield的持股增加了17.52%，新增611.1万股，总持股量达到4100.5万股，总价值约21.5亿美元，成为投资组合第二大持仓，占比达到18.01%。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">谷歌A持股新增45.1万股，增幅为11.33%，总持股达到443.8万股，价值约6.86亿美元。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">值得注意的是，Ackman还增加了对赫兹租车（Hertz Global Holdings）的持股，增幅达18%。这一举动是4月通过更新的SEC文件披露的，原本因保密请求而未在第四季度持股文件中显示的1270万股购买现已公开。</p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">重大减持：希尔顿遭大幅削减，谷歌C类股不被看好</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">除了清仓耐克之外，在减持方面，Ackman对希尔顿环球控股(Hilton Worldwide Holdings)进行了大幅裁减，减少了243.98万股，降幅高达44.84%。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">此外，Ackman还减持了谷歌C类股票122.36万股，减持幅度16.21%。值得注意的是，年初至今，谷歌C类股票下跌13.05%</p><p>本文来自华尔街见闻，欢迎下载APP查看更多</p>

<h2 style="text-align: left;">重大清仓：部分科技股和中概股遭抛售</h2>
<p>在一季度中，索罗斯基金完全清仓了78只股票，减持45只股票。</p>
<p><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/79ea4c63-4cae-4e8a-8c17-e52dec454f2a.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="473" data-wscnw="622"/></p>
<p>除了前面提到的小型股、超微电脑和台积电外，索罗斯基金还完全清仓了：<strong>保险公司Arthur J. Gallagher的所有300,000股、Airbnb、阿里巴巴和波音的所有持股</strong>。</p>
<p><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/7752a52b-c520-446b-8f7d-66f122cd265e.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="415" data-wscnw="1016"/></p>
<p><strong>减持方面</strong>，索罗斯基金减持了38只股票。最显著的变化包括：</p>
<blockquote>
<ul>
<li>谷歌母公司Alphabet的833,923股，减持幅度达62.64%，对投资组合影响为-3.24%。</li>
</ul>

<ul>
<li>同时还大幅减持京东2,561,320股，减持幅度高达93.6%，对投资组合影响为-1.83%。</li>

Title:  当下最火的问题：美股反弹到头了吗？摩根大通市场部门：还没有，这真让人痛苦 
Description:  <p style="text-align: left;">在经历了令人印象深刻的反弹之后，投资者们不禁要问，这次市场反弹是否已经结束？</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">摩根大通的市场部门负责人Andrew Tyler的回答是：尚未结束。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">在最新发布的报告中，Tyler表示，<strong>当前牛市的核心要素仍然存在（宏观数据具有韧性，盈利向好，以及贸易形势缓和），但复苏的速度使得这次上涨成为“最不受欢迎”的上涨之一，增量买盘主要来自散户投资者和企业。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Tyler还指出，虽然回调的风险正在上升，但再次回调的可能性不大。 集中度风险将加剧对回调风险的担忧，因为这可能与去年上半年的市场类似，主要集中于大型科技股上，<strong>预计标普500指数本季度应该会触及历史高点（6144点）。</strong></p>
<h2><strong>收益率上升推动投资者转向股市</strong></h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">摩根大通分析了市场对10年期美债收益率突破4.5%的反应。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">Tyler认为，这并不完全是负面因素。他的同事John Schlegel发现，<strong>在后疫情时代，当10年期美债收益率创出新高时，股市需要1-2个月的时间来消化和调整。周期高点为5%，所以仍有一定上涨空间。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><strong><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/8413eac1-0301-4c68-b17f-bb21fe712f19.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="273" data-wscnw="500"/></strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">摩根大通进一步指出<strong>，</strong>收益率的上升应会促使投资者转向高质量的股票，尤其是大型科技股，同时对必需消费品和公用事业板块构成压力。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">该行的利率策略师还将其2025年底的10年期国债收益率预测从4%上调至4.35%，将2年期美债收益率预测从3.1%上调至3.5%。这种收益率预测的增加源于更高的实际GDP增长预期和更低的通胀预期，导致降息周期推迟且幅度减小。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">尽管市场大幅反弹，但机构持仓情况却显示出不同的图景——对冲基金仍在净卖出，杠杆率仍接近历史低点，市场情绪低迷。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">摩根大通认为，<strong>由于市场仍存在大量怀疑情绪，如果这次反弹真的能持续，那么可能会出现意想不到的情况。</strong></p>
<h2><strong>市场情绪较为乐观，Mag 7正在回归“旧模式”</strong></h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">摩根大通交易部门的其他成员也分享了他们的看法：</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">TMT部门的Brian Heavey指出，活跃的多头投资者似乎配置不足，表现逊于标普500指数的上涨。尽管如此，并未看到大规模的“追高”——该行实际上在昨天净卖出科技股。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">随着更广泛的指数收复关键支撑位，CTA开始成为更好的买家，股票回购增加，任何短期下跌都可能较浅，因为主动型资金将被迫重新进入更高风险/更高贝塔值的股票。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">工业部门的Paige Hanson指出，市场反弹在周二表现出工业板块的韧性，之前由于工业板块和周期性股票的空头回补，这些股票在周一表现不佳；然而，周二，<strong>这些被看好的周期性股票出现补涨，表现优于大盘，且反弹更加健康，这表明主动增持而非被动回补。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">同时，根据摩根大通近日在波士顿举办的科技大会的反馈，尽管化工/运输/住房等股票的市场波动较大，但目前尚未听到市场情绪发生实质性变化，<strong>企业和投资者对宏观/市场持相当乐观的态度。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">消费部门的Briggs Barton表示，最新数据显示，截至5月6日，<strong>消费者支出数据表现积极。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">期货期权部门的Marissa Gitler指出，<strong>Mag 7正在回归“旧模式”，即当对宏观前景感到担忧时，可以买入“安全”的大型科技股。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">年初，资金从美国风险资产流出，主要集中在Mag7。现在，资金回流美国风险资产，似乎正在逆转这一趋势并缩小差距。</p><p>本文来自华尔街见闻，欢迎下载APP查看更多</p> 

Title:  巴菲特、索罗斯、盖茨，看看大佬们买了点啥 
Description:  <p>每季度的13F报告，都是投资者观察顶级资金动向的窗口。一季度，在关税风暴来临前，华尔街大佬们做了哪些调整？</p>
<p><strong>从巴菲特“持币不买“的策略，到索罗斯的&#34;抛小盘买大盘&#34;，再到桥水的黄金配置，都显示出顶级投资者正在增强组合的防御性，而嗅觉灵敏的Ackman更是赶在关税前清仓了耐克。</strong></p>
<p><strong>对科技股态度出现分化</strong>，巴菲特依然坚守苹果，索罗斯加仓英伟达、清仓超微电脑，桥水则减持了谷歌、英伟达、Meta等。</p>
<p><strong>对于中概股</strong>，曾豪言“买入一切中国资产”的对冲基金大佬David Tepper谨慎乐观，虽减持阿里，但仍维持其第一大持仓的地位。桥水则果断加码，一季度新进京东持仓，增持阿里巴巴、百度。</p>
<h2><strong>巴菲特：坚守苹果，清仓花旗，持币不买</strong></h2>
<p>&#34;股神&#34;依然保持着他标志性的稳健风格。</p>
<p>本季最大变化是彻底清仓了花旗集团，同时减持了部分金融股，这或许反映了巴菲特对传统金融业前景的谨慎态度。</p>
<p><a href="https://wallstreetcn.com/articles/3747202" target="_blank" rel="noopener">据监管文件显示</a>，伯克希尔出售了全部1464万股花旗集团股票，减持了4866万股美国银行股票（减持比例超过7%），以及减持了30万股第一资本金融公司股票（约4%）。</p>
<p>不过，巴菲特的核心持仓依然稳如磐石：苹果继续占据首位，持有3亿股，约占投资组合的25%，市值高达666亿美元；美国运通紧随其后，可口可乐稳居第三，此外还有美银和雪佛龙。</p>
<p>值得一提的是，伯克希尔向SEC提出了保密请求，要求对一项或多项股票投资暂不披露。媒体分析认为，如果此次保密持仓仅为一只股票，其投资规模可能在10亿至20亿美元之间，很可能属于&#34;商业与工业类&#34;股票。</p>
<p>在市场波动加剧的环境下，巴菲特的&#34;不买，还是不买&#34;策略似乎在传递一个信号——当前充满不确定性的市场环境下，现金或许是更好的选择。</p>
<h2><strong>索罗斯：转向防守姿态</strong></h2>
<p>索罗斯基金在一季度作出了防御性调整，增持标普500指数，同时减持罗素2000小盘股指数。</p>
<p>根据最新提交的13F文件显示，索罗斯基金管理公司一季度完全清仓了iShares罗素2000ETF(IWM)的所有75.2万股持仓，此举对其投资组合产生了-3.41%的影响。</p>
<p>与此同时，大幅增持了SPDR标普500ETF(SPY)，新增19.6万股，使总持股量达到31.4万股，增幅高达165.93%，对当前投资组合的影响达2.17%，总价值约1.76亿美元。</p>
<p>在经济增长放缓、利率高企的背景下，小型企业往往比大型企业面临更大的经营压力。索罗斯&#34;买大盘、抛小盘&#34;操作或许表明，他认为未来市场波动中，大型企业的抗风险能力将更胜一筹。</p>
<h2><strong>桥水：买入3亿美元黄金，看多新兴市场，减持谷歌和英伟达</strong></h2>
<p>由Ray Dalio创立、目前管理约920亿美元资产的桥水基金，在一季度进行了大幅度的投资组合重构。最引人注目的是，<a href="https://wallstreetcn.com/articles/3747111" target="_blank" rel="noopener">桥水新建立了SPDR黄金ETF的大型头寸</a>，买入3亿美元黄金ETF（GLD），成为其美股第六大持仓。</p>
<p><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/7461d868-6977-4361-954b-a1cdea016873.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="353" data-wscnw="1024"/></p>
<p>与此同时，桥水虽然将其最大的多头头寸SPDR标普500 ETF削减了一半以上，但小幅增持了iShares核心标普500 ETF和iShares核心MSCI新兴市场ETF。这表明桥水虽然降低了对美国市场的总体敞口，但仍看好市场整体走势，并更加关注新兴市场机会。</p>
<p>桥水还增持了阿里巴巴集团540多万股，使其总持股量达到约566万股，并将在线旅游代理Booking Holdings和云计算巨头Salesforce的持仓翻了一番以上。</p>
<p>反观其对&#34;华尔街七巨头&#34;中的谷歌和英伟达却分别减持了约15%和18%以上，显示出对科技巨头的谨慎态度。</p>
<h2><strong>Ackman</strong><strong>：清仓耐克，提前预判关税风险</strong></h2>
<p>Bill Ackman一季度的第一个大动作是重仓Uber。他旗下的潘兴广场资本在一季度增持了3030万股Uber，总市值22.1亿美元，使其一跃成为第一大持仓股（占比18.5%）。</p>
<p>更值得关注的是，他在美国宣布 “对等关税”之前，完全清仓了此前持有的1876.9万股耐克，预判到耐克这类高度全球化企业，在新一轮贸易政策中将首当其冲，展现出强大的政策敏感度。</p>
<h2><strong>Tepper</strong><strong>：减持阿里，但中国科技股调整仍占主导</strong></h2>
<p>曾豪言“买入一切中国资产”的<a href="https://wallstreetcn.com/articles/3747216" target="_blank" rel="noopener">对冲基金大佬David Tepper</a>一季度减持了261万股阿里巴巴，持股减少了22.06%。</p>
<p>然而，阿里和拼多多仍然稳居其投资组合的前两位，占比分别达14.56%和6.17%，第五位的京东占比也达到3.95%，意味着中概股在其前五大持仓中占据了三席，反映了Tepper对中国资产的谨慎乐观情绪。</p>
<p><img class=" wscnph" src="https://wpimg-wscn.awtmt.com/6f94d4ef-dc26-4563-9366-1ab986f8e428.png" data-wscntype="image" data-wscnh="739" data-wscnw="1024"/></p>
<p>除了减持阿里巴巴，Tepper还减持了46万股微软，持股减少47.42%，并完全清空了AMD的120万股持仓以及联邦快递的35万股。</p>
<p>Tepper去年9月曾表示，中国市场的股票比美股便宜太多，且中国推出宽松政策的力度超预期，这种&#34;调整但仍占主导&#34;的策略，展示了他对中国数字经济的长期信心依然存在。</p>
<h2><strong>Renaissance</strong><strong>：大幅增持Robinhood，看好加密货币前景</strong></h2>
<p>由已故Jim Simons创立的Renaissance Technologies今年开局强劲，其旗下Renaissance机构股票基金截至4月上涨12.73%，Renaissance机构多元化Alpha基金上涨16.75%。</p>
<p>尽管Renaissance没有改变其顶级持仓，但做了一些调整。该公司将其最大的美国股票多头头寸Palantir Technologies减持了28%以上。Palantir在季末约占其美国普通股资产的2%。</p>
<p>Renaissance在前三个月最重要的多头押注是将Robinhood Markets的持股增加了37%以上，使其成为公司第四大美国普通股多头头寸。</p>
<p>Robinhood正继续深入加密货币市场，特别是在周二宣布已同意收购加拿大的WonderFi Technologies之后。这一增持可能表明Renaissance看好加密货币交易平台的发展前景。</p>
<h2><strong>盖茨：巴菲特退休前，减持伯克希尔</strong></h2>
<p>虽然比尔·盖茨并非专业投资人，但这位老牌首富的投资组合同样值得关注。</p>
<p>盖茨的三大持仓依次为微软、伯克希尔·哈撒韦和废弃物管理公司——这个组合既有高科技，也有价值投资和必需服务，显示出他对投资多元化的重视。</p>
<p>值得注意的是，盖茨在一季度小幅减持了伯克希尔·哈撒韦的股份。这是否意味着他对巴菲特退休后公司的发展前景持有疑虑？</p><p>本文来自华尔街见闻，欢迎下载APP查看更多</p> 

Title:  大幅扩赤字，共和党这份减税案，美债吃得消吗？ 
Description:  <p style="text-align: left;">美国税改法案将在未来十年增加至少4万亿美元赤字，恐给债市埋下“大雷”。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">在全球贸易局势缓和之际，美国国内财政问题正悄然酝酿更大风险。据追风交易台消息，巴克莱银行的最新研报显示，<strong>众议院版本的税改法案实际将为美国十年赤字增加约4万亿美元。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">而当前，美国的赤字已处于GDP的6.5-7%的水平，这一法案将进一步推高长期美债收益率，给债券投资者带来沉重打击。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">随着关税问题暂告段落，投资者的焦点转向正在国会推进的税改法案，以及它对美国长期赤字可能带来的影响。</p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">赤字注定继续攀升，法案将在未来十年增加4万亿美元赤字</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">根据巴克莱的分析，美国财政政策长期存在的问题之一是国会通过“花招”使赤字预测显得更小，比如减税通常前置，削减支出则后置。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">目前的税改法案延续了这一趋势。据媒体报道，周二，众议院筹款委员会标注了该法案的众议院版本，<strong>该法案延长了即将到期的《减税与就业法案》(TCJA)，从而在未来十年内增加了近3.8万亿美元的赤字(超过现行法律)；它还通过增加国防和移民执法支出，增加了3200亿美元的赤字。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">法案通过各种措施回收了1万亿美元税收，如取消电动汽车税收抵免、IRA抵免、《平价医疗法案》支付等。但随后，该法案又通过总统要求的新减税——如不对小费和加班征税、提高标准扣除额等——增加了1万亿美元赤字。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">在支出方面，该法案计划在未来十年减少1.3-1.4万亿美元支出。其中大部分(超过9000亿美元)应该来自能源和商业委员会，该委员会对医疗补助拥有唯一管辖权，对医疗保险拥有联合管辖权。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">报告称，如果参议院没有改变现有措辞，该法案似乎会在未来十年增加约2.5万亿美元赤字(不包括利息支出)。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">更令人担忧的是，新提出的减税措施预计于2028年到期，这使成本“仅”为1万亿美元。但历史经验表明，<strong>国会很可能在2029年延长这些到期的减税措施。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">负责任联邦预算委员会(CFRB)估计，将这些新减税延长十年——这可能是进行这项分析的正确方式——将为美国赤字增加另外1.5万亿美元。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><strong>这意味着，该法案在未来十年的成本膨胀到4万亿美元。</strong>随着利息支付也将上升(随着低成本债务的到期)，美国债务/GDP状况在未来几年“似乎没有稳定的机会”。</p>
<h2>美国财政状况不容乐观</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;">根据巴克莱的报告，2024财年，美国借款规模超过2.5万亿美元，到2025财年，这一数字似乎有望降至2.1万亿美元以下。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">报告称，从数字上看这是一种改善，<strong>但这一数字被美联储减少量化紧缩的事实美化了。</strong>随着今年从美联储资产负债表上到期的债券减少，财政部的借款需求表面上有所改善，此外还有财政部现金余额变化的问题。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;"><strong>在考虑这两个变量后，报告称，2025财年美国财政部的真实融资需求接近2万亿美元，略高于2024财年。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">报告指出，考虑到美国税法是累进制的，当经济增长放缓时，税收往往会低于预期，如果2025年美国经济增长明显弱于去年，赤字将进一步扩大。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">报告总结称，<strong>即使不考虑税改法案落地带来的赤字增加，在经济良好时期，美国的赤字似乎“注定”至少要占GDP的6.5-7.0%；在经济衰退的情况下，赤字将大幅增加。</strong></p>
<h2 style="text-align: left;">债券市场“瑟瑟发抖”</h2>
<p style="text-align: left;"><span style="font-size: 16px; color: #333333;">报告指出，尽管今年的GDP增长肯定会低于2024年，美国债券收益率却已连续数周上升。这部分是因为对2025年通胀的担忧，部分是由于外国投资者将一些资本从美国资产转移出去，部分是因为在关税缓和后，经济衰退的风险似乎已经避免。</span></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">但长期利率期限溢价上升的部分原因可能是因为美国没有努力控制不断上升的赤字。</p>
<p style="text-align: left;">报告进一步指出，<strong>投资者越担心赤字，长期利率就越高，这推高了未来的利息支付，反过来又导致债券投资者更加担忧——如此循环往复。</strong></p>
<p style="text-align: left;">美国长期债券已接近自全球金融危机以来的最高水平。随着市场消化新税改法案的细节，并意识到赤字很可能在可预见的未来继续上升，长期收益率也有继续上升的风险。</p>




<p>本文来自华尔街见闻，欢迎下载APP查看更多</p> 

Title:  高瓴旗下HHLR一季度加码中国资产，新进百度、理想，重仓拼多多

<p>周四公布的一季度机构投资者持仓13F报告显示，高瓴旗下独立且专注于二级市场投资的基金管理平台HHLR Advisors一季度新进和增持了近20只中概股。包括<strong>新进买入了百度、理想等10家公司，以及增持了富途控股、拼多多、网易、京东、携程等8家公司</strong>，减持阿里巴巴和百济神州等公司。</p>
<p>根据数据披露<strong>，HHLR一季度持仓总市值从上季末的28.9亿美元增至35.4亿美元，增幅接近23%</strong>。总体数据显示，该基金今年一季度前十大持仓标的占比88.46%。总共增持8只股票、减持10只股票、新进11只股票、清仓7只股票。</p>
